### **MODULE 2: PERMITTED USES AND DISCLOSURES FRAMEWORK (75 MIN)**

#### **1\) Entry-Level Primer**

**Plain Language Summary:** Not every request for PHI is allowed. You must understand which disclosures are permitted before sharing any PHI. The framework is: Treatment, Payment, Operations (TPO) disclosures may be permitted by client policy; patient/authorized representative requests are generally permitted; all other requests require authorization on file or escalation. Your role is NOT to independently decide what's allowed — you follow client policy, check for authorization, and escalate when unclear. Mistakes in disclosure decisions cause breaches.

**Observable Behaviors:**

* Identifies requester type (patient, payer, provider, family, internal, other)  
* Identifies stated purpose (treatment, payment, operations, unknown)  
* Checks client policy or authorization registry before disclosing  
* Refuses disclosure using approved script when not clearly permitted  
* Escalates ambiguous requests with minimal necessary context  
* Documents disclosure decision basis without extra PHI

#### **2\) Definitions**

**TPO (Treatment, Payment, Operations):** Three categories of healthcare activities for which PHI disclosures may be permitted without patient authorization under HIPAA, subject to client-specific policies (needs\_review).

**Treatment:** Activities related to providing, coordinating, or managing healthcare and related services. Examples: provider consultations, referrals, care coordination.

**Payment:** Activities to obtain reimbursement for healthcare services. Examples: billing, claims processing, payment posting, collections, benefits verification, prior authorization.

**Operations:** Administrative, quality, and business activities. Examples: quality improvement, case management, training (with de-identified data), audits, legal/compliance.

**Business Associate (needs\_review):** Entity performing services for covered entity involving PHI access (payers, clearinghouses, vendors). Business associate agreements govern permitted disclosures; client-specific BA lists need\_review.

**Authorization:** Written permission from patient (or legal representative) specifying what PHI can be disclosed, to whom, for what purpose, and expiration date. Must be on file before disclosure for non-TPO purposes.

**Representative:** Individual legally authorized to act on patient's behalf (legal guardian, healthcare power of attorney, parent of minor, executor of estate). Authorization status needs\_review per client procedures.

**Escalation Trigger:** Situation requiring supervisor or privacy officer review before disclosure decision (ambiguous purpose, missing authorization, conflicting information, unusual request).

#### **3\) Step-by-Step Procedures**

**Procedure: Evaluate Disclosure Requests**

1. Identify requester type:

   * Patient themselves (photo ID verification at minimum)  
   * Patient's authorized representative (requires authorization verification — needs\_review per client)  
   * Payer (insurance company, claims processor)  
   * Provider (referring physician, treating facility, consultant)  
   * Family member (not automatically authorized)  
   * Internal staff (must have need-to-know for role)  
   * Other (attorney, employer, media, law enforcement, etc.)  
2. Identify stated purpose:

   * Treatment (care coordination, referral, consultation)  
   * Payment (billing, claim, coverage, prior auth)  
   * Operations (quality, audit, case management)  
   * Patient request (patient accessing own information)  
   * Other/unclear  
3. Check applicable authority:

   * For patient requests: verify identity using Module 4 procedures  
   * For payer requests: confirm client policy allows disclosure for stated payment purpose (needs\_review per client); many require verification of payer identity  
   * For provider requests: confirm treatment relationship and verify provider identity (needs\_review per client procedures)  
   * For family/representative requests: check authorization on file before proceeding  
   * For other requests: authorization required unless specific law mandates disclosure  
4. Make disclosure decision:

   * If clearly permitted by client policy AND identity verified AND minimum necessary determined: proceed with disclosure  
   * If not clearly permitted by client policy: refuse using approved script, offer alternative (e.g., patient can authorize disclosure), escalate if requester persists  
   * If authorization required but not on file: refuse, explain how to submit authorization  
   * If uncertain about any element: escalate before disclosing (safe stop Rule 3 and 5\)  
5. Document decision basis:

   * Requester type and verified identity  
   * Stated purpose  
   * Policy basis for disclosure or refusal  
   * What was disclosed (minimum necessary items)  
   * If refused: refusal reason and alternative offered  
   * If escalated: escalation timestamp and decision outcome

**Procedure: Refuse Disclosure Safely**

1. Listen to full request without interrupting  
2. Acknowledge request: "I understand you're requesting \[information type\]."  
3. State refusal clearly and professionally: "I'm unable to provide that information without \[authorization/verification/policy confirmation\]."  
4. Explain reason briefly: "Our policy requires \[specific requirement\] before we can share this information."  
5. Offer alternative: "You can \[submit written authorization/contact the patient to request release/verify identity through this process\]."  
6. If requester argues or pressures: repeat refusal calmly, do not deviate from script  
7. If pressure continues (safe stop Rule 4): "I need to escalate this request to my supervisor. Please hold or I can call you back within \[timeframe\]."  
8. End interaction professionally: "Is there anything else I can help with that doesn't require this information?"  
9. Document: requester details, request, refusal reason, alternative offered, any pressure tactics used

**Safe Stop Rules:**

* Stop if stated purpose is unclear or changes during conversation — escalate  
* Stop if requester cannot or will not verify identity — refuse disclosure  
* Stop if authorization status is uncertain — escalate before disclosing  
* Stop if you are being pressured to disclose "just this once" — maintain refusal, escalate

**Escalation Path:** Supervisor → Privacy officer (for disclosure decision confirmation)

**Documentation Requirements:**

* All disclosure decisions documented in disclosure log or ticket with: requester, purpose, authority basis, what disclosed, date/time  
* Refusals documented with reason and alternative offered  
* Escalations documented with decision outcome

#### **4\) Worked Examples**

**Compliant Example 1 \- Spouse Request (Source Scenario 2):** Caller: "This is John Smith. I need my wife Sarah's test results. We're married."

Employee: "I understand you'd like Sarah's test results. Our policy requires authorization from Sarah before I can share her information with anyone else, including spouses. Is Sarah available to speak with me directly, or she can complete an authorization form allowing us to speak with you."

Caller: "She's at work. Can't you just tell me?"

Employee: "I'm unable to share without her authorization. Sarah can call us directly, or if she completes an authorization form naming you, we can discuss her information with you. Would you like me to email her the authorization form instructions at the address we have on file?"

**Why Compliant:** Correctly identified spouse is not automatically authorized, refused disclosure using script, offered alternatives (patient calls directly or submits authorization), maintained refusal despite pressure, documented interaction.

**Compliant Example 2 \- Payer Request (needs\_review for client-specific):** Payer representative calls: "This is Claims Department at Blue Cross calling about claim 123456 for patient account 789\. We need the office visit notes to process the claim."

Employee: "I need to verify your identity first. May I have your name, department, and a callback number at Blue Cross?" \[Verifies using client procedure for payer verification — needs\_review\]. "Thank you. Let me confirm our policy allows this disclosure for claims processing purposes." \[Checks policy, confirms payment purpose disclosure permitted\]. "I can send the office visit notes via \[approved method — needs\_review\]. The fax number on file is \[verifies number\]. You should receive within one business day."

**Why Compliant:** Verified payer identity per procedure, confirmed client policy permits payment-purpose disclosure, used approved transmission method, applied minimum necessary (only office visit notes requested, not entire medical record), documented disclosure.

**Non-Compliant Example:** Family member calls: "Hi, this is Dr. Johnson's daughter. He was admitted yesterday and I'm out of state. Can you tell me what his diagnosis is and when he can go home?"

Employee: "Oh sure, let me look... He has pneumonia and should be discharged tomorrow if his oxygen levels improve."

**Why Non-Compliant:** Multiple violations:

* Did not verify identity (family member may not be who they claim)  
* Did not check for authorization on file before disclosing to family member  
* Disclosed diagnosis and treatment plan without confirming daughter is authorized representative  
* Did not verify patient wants daughter to have this information  
* Source Scenario 2 directly addresses this pattern

**Correct Response:** "I understand you're concerned about your father. Our policy requires me to verify with him that he authorizes me to share his medical information with you. Is he available to speak with me now? Alternatively, he can complete an authorization form that allows us to speak with specific family members."

#### **5\) Practice Pack**

**Drill Set: Approve/Deny/Escalate Flashcards (15 Scenarios)**

Classify each request as: A) Approve (clearly permitted), D) Deny (clearly prohibited), E) Escalate (uncertain)

1. Patient requests own test results after verifying identity → A (patient right to access)  
2. Spouse requests diagnosis without authorization on file → D (family not automatically authorized)  
3. Payer requests claim documentation for payment processing, policy confirms permitted → A (payment purpose, policy allows)  
4. Provider requests records for treatment, but caller identity not verified → E (purpose may be permitted but identity verification incomplete)  
5. Employer requests employee fitness-for-duty certification → E (requires authorization unless specific workers' comp or required by law)  
6. Patient's attorney requests records, no authorization on file → D (requires patient authorization)  
7. Patient's mother requests information, patient is 35 years old → D (adult patient, parent not automatically authorized)  
8. Internal staff member requests access, but purpose is unclear → E (internal access requires need-to-know; unclear purpose triggers escalation)  
9. Law enforcement requests records without warrant or subpoena → E (requires legal review of specific circumstances — needs\_review)  
10. Patient requests records be sent to new provider, identity verified → A (patient direction of own information)  
11. Insurance broker "helping patient" requests coverage details → D (broker not automatically authorized; patient must request directly or provide authorization)  
12. Collection agency requests patient contact information for debt collection → E (payment purpose but requires verification of BA status and client policy — needs\_review)  
13. Researcher requests de-identified data for study → E (requires privacy officer review of de-identification adequacy and IRB approval)  
14. Patient's legal guardian requests information (guardianship papers on file) → A (legal guardian is authorized representative)  
15. Media requests information about patient involved in news story → D (no disclosure without patient authorization)

**Scoring:** 80% \= 12/15 correct

**Scenario Exercise:** **Scenario:** Three-party call: patient, patient's insurance case manager, and you. Patient verified identity at call start. Case manager asks about upcoming surgery details to coordinate post-op care. Patient is silent. What do you do?

**Answer:**

1. Acknowledge request: "I understand you're asking about the surgery to coordinate care."  
2. Verify patient consent in real-time: "Mr./Ms. \[Patient\], are you still on the line? Do you authorize me to discuss your surgery details with \[Case Manager Name\] from \[Insurance Company\] for care coordination purposes?"  
3. If patient confirms verbally: "Thank you. \[Proceeds with disclosure to case manager\]"  
4. If patient is uncertain or silent: "Let me give you and \[Case Manager\] a moment. Mr./Ms. \[Patient\], you can call me back directly if you'd like to discuss this privately first."  
5. Document: Three-party call, patient verbal authorization obtained (or not obtained), what was disclosed

**Why Correct:** Even though patient initiated the call and verified identity, explicit verbal consent required before discussing PHI with third party on line. Patient silence is not consent.

**Mini-Simulation Script:** **Trainer plays:** Caller requesting information  
 **Learner plays:** Employee evaluating disclosure request

**Round 1 \- Pressure Scenario:** **Trainer:** "This is the billing office at Northside Clinic. We need patient insurance information for Jane Doe immediately. She's here now waiting to be seen and forgot her card."

**Learner expected actions:**

1. Identify requester type: provider office (treatment context)  
2. Verify identity: "May I have your name, the clinic's phone number, and Jane Doe's date of birth?"  
3. Verify patient presence: "Is Jane available to speak with me directly to authorize this disclosure?" OR "Can Jane verify her identity and give permission?"  
4. If Jane verifies and authorizes: provide insurance information  
5. If Jane cannot verify or clinic resists: "I need Jane to verify directly or we can send information to the clinic through our standard records release process."  
6. If pressured: "I understand this is urgent for her appointment, but I need to follow our verification procedure to protect Jane's information."

**Round 2 \- Ambiguous Purpose:** **Trainer:** "Hi, I'm calling from Community Health Partners about one of your patients. Can you tell me what appointments they have coming up?"

**Learner expected actions:**

1. Identify unclear elements: Which patient? What is specific purpose? Is this treatment coordination or something else?  
2. Clarify: "Can you tell me the patient's name and the specific reason you need their appointment information?"  
3. Assess stated purpose against TPO framework  
4. If treatment coordination and identity verifiable: may proceed if policy allows  
5. If purpose remains unclear: "I need to escalate this request to verify our policy allows this disclosure. May I call you back at \[verified number\]?"  
6. Escalate with minimal context: Document requester, vague purpose, need for policy clarification

**Checklist: Disclosure Decision Process**

* \[ \] Identified requester type (patient/representative/payer/provider/family/internal/other)  
* \[ \] Identified stated purpose (treatment/payment/operations/patient access/other/unclear)  
* \[ \] Verified requester identity using appropriate method (Module 4 procedures)  
* \[ \] Checked client policy or authorization on file  
* \[ \] If unclear: escalated before disclosing  
* \[ \] If denying: used refusal script, offered alternative  
* \[ \] If approving: applied minimum necessary  
* \[ \] Documented decision basis completely

**Scoring Rubric:**

* Requester type identification: 1 point  
* Purpose identification: 1 point  
* Identity verification: 2 points  
* Policy/authorization check: 2 points  
* Decision logic: 2 points  
* Documentation: 2 points  
* **Total: 10 points (8/10 \= 80% passing)**

#### **6\) Knowledge Check (15 Items)**

**Item 1 (MCQ):** TPO stands for: A) Transfer, Payment, Operations  
 B) Treatment, Privacy, Operations  
 C) Treatment, Payment, Operations  
 D) Transfer, Privacy, Oversight

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** TPO \= Treatment, Payment, Operations.

**Item 2 (MCQ):** A spouse requests patient's diagnosis. What is required before disclosure? A) Nothing; spouses are automatically authorized  
 B) Authorization from patient on file or patient present to authorize  
 C) Marriage certificate  
 D) Only if patient is incapacitated

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Spouses are not automatically authorized; requires patient authorization.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — Source Scenario 2 directly addresses this misconception.

**Item 3 (True/False):** If a provider calls requesting treatment-related information, you can disclose immediately without verification.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Must verify caller identity and confirm client policy allows treatment disclosures to that provider.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — forgetting identity verification requirement.

**Item 4 (MCQ):** Your role in disclosure decisions is to: A) Independently decide what's in patient's best interest  
 B) Follow client policy, check authorizations, escalate when uncertain  
 C) Always default to denying requests  
 D) Defer all decisions to supervisor

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Staff follow policy and escalate; do not make independent judgments.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — exceeding role boundaries.

**Item 5 (Scenario MCQ):** Patient's adult child calls asking about mother's medication list. Mother is 68 years old and competent. What do you do? A) Provide medication list; adult children are authorized  
 B) Refuse; check for authorization on file first  
 C) Ask mother's permission verbally over phone  
 D) Provide general information but not specifics

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Adult children not automatically authorized for competent adult patients.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — assuming family relationship creates authorization.

**Item 6 (MCQ):** Authorization on file must specify: A) Only patient name  
 B) What information, to whom, purpose, expiration date  
 C) Only who can receive information  
 D) Verbal permission is sufficient

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Authorization requires specific elements: what, to whom, purpose, expiration.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** D — authorization must be written.

**Item 7 (True/False):** If a caller becomes angry and demands information, you should disclose to de-escalate the situation.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Pressure does not override disclosure rules; safe stop Rule 4 applies.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — prioritizing conflict avoidance over compliance.

**Item 8 (MCQ):** Business Associate disclosures require: A) Patient authorization for each disclosure  
 B) Business associate agreement on file and permitted purpose  
 C) Only verbal agreement  
 D) No special requirements

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** BA disclosures require BAA and legitimate purpose (needs\_review per client).  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — TPO disclosures to BAs don't require individual authorizations if BAA covers.

**Item 9 (Scenario MCQ):** Payer requests claim documentation. You check policy and confirm payment-purpose disclosure is permitted. What else must you do before disclosing? A) Nothing; proceed with disclosure  
 B) Verify payer identity and apply minimum necessary  
 C) Get patient authorization  
 D) Wait for supervisor approval

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Must verify requester identity and apply minimum necessary even for permitted disclosures.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — identity verification still required.

**Item 10 (MCQ):** When refusing disclosure, you should: A) Hang up to avoid argument  
 B) Use refusal script, explain reason briefly, offer alternative  
 C) Explain detailed legal reasons  
 D) Transfer to supervisor immediately

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Professional refusal includes script, brief reason, alternative.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** D — escalation not needed for straightforward refusals.

**Item 11 (True/False):** "I'm just checking on my neighbor" is a sufficient purpose for disclosure.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** "Just checking" is insufficient purpose; also no authorization (safe stop Rule 3).  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — not recognizing insufficient purpose.

**Item 12 (MCQ):** Patient requests records be sent to attorney. What do you need? A) Attorney's bar number  
 B) Signed authorization from patient specifying attorney and purpose  
 C) Patient verbal permission  
 D) Court order

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Patient direction requires written authorization specifying recipient and purpose.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — verbal permission insufficient for third-party disclosure.

**Item 13 (Scenario MCQ):** Internal staff member asks for patient information. They say "I need it for my work." What do you do? A) Provide; they're internal so it's fine  
 B) Ask specific purpose, verify need-to-know, check if they have appropriate access rights  
 C) Deny; internal staff must access systems themselves  
 D) Provide if they're management level

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Internal access requires need-to-know verification and appropriate permissions.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — "internal" doesn't eliminate need-to-know requirement.

**Item 14 (Short Answer):** What are the three escalation triggers that should cause you to stop and escalate before making disclosure decision?

**Answer:**

1. Ambiguous or unclear purpose  
2. Missing or uncertain authorization status  
3. Unusual or complex request outside normal workflow

**Rationale:** These situations require privacy officer or supervisor review.

**Item 15 (MCQ):** Documentation of disclosure decisions must include: A) Only what was disclosed  
 B) Requester, purpose, authority basis, what disclosed, date/time  
 C) Only if disclosure was denied  
 D) Patient's medical information for context

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Complete disclosure documentation includes all decision elements.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — both approvals and denials must be documented.

#### **7\) QA and Audit Hooks**

**Evidence of Competence:**

* Disclosure decision justifications (10 scenarios, scored on decision logic and documentation)  
* Flashcard drill score (15 items, 12/15 \= 80%)  
* Knowledge check score (15 items, 12/15 \= 80%)  
* Simulation performance scored on refusal script use and pressure handling

**Supervisor Review Checklist:**

* \[ \] Learner correctly identifies requester types and purposes  
* \[ \] Learner verifies identity before disclosure  
* \[ \] Learner checks policy/authorization before proceeding  
* \[ \] Learner escalates appropriately (not over-escalating or under-escalating)  
* \[ \] Learner uses refusal script professionally  
* \[ \] Learner documents decision basis completely

**Recordkeeping Tie-Back:**

* Module 2 completion with artifact submissions logged  
* All scenario scores stored  
* Module 2 completion date and scores recorded as required training record fields

---

### **MODULE 3: MINIMUM NECESSARY AND ACCESS CONTROLS (90 MIN)**

#### **1\) Entry-Level Primer**

**Plain Language Summary:** Minimum Necessary is the cornerstone of PHI protection: only access, use, and share what you need for your specific task. Most access violations are not hackers — they're employees accessing more than necessary, browsing out of curiosity, or failing to remove unused permissions. This module trains least privilege (narrowest access needed for role), preventing access creep (accumulating unnecessary permissions over time), recognizing curiosity browsing (accessing records without job need), requesting access formally with justification, and auditing your own permissions periodically.

**Observable Behaviors:**

* States task purpose before opening any record  
* Opens only minimum screens/fields required for task  
* Never accesses records of known individuals without job need  
* Files formal access requests with written justification when current access insufficient  
* Conducts periodic self-audit of access permissions  
* Closes record immediately if curiosity temptation arises

#### **2\) Definitions**

**Least Privilege:** Security principle requiring users have only minimum access rights needed to perform assigned duties. No more, no less.

**Role-Based Access:** Access permissions granted based on job role, not individual requests. Common in healthcare systems (e.g., "billing clerk" role has specific permissions).

**Access Creep:** Gradual accumulation of unnecessary access permissions over time, usually from role changes, temporary assignments, or failure to remove old permissions.

**Curiosity Browsing:** Accessing patient records without legitimate job need, driven by personal interest (celebrity patients, neighbors, family members, coworkers, newsworthy cases). Serious violation often resulting in termination.

**Need-to-Know:** Legitimate job requirement to access specific information to perform assigned duties. "Nice to know" or "want to know" is not sufficient.

**Access Request:** Formal documented request for additional system permissions, including justification of job need and scope of access requested.

**Access Removal:** Formal documented removal of permissions no longer needed (after role change, project completion, or identified during self-audit).

**Audit Trail:** System log recording who accessed what information, when, and from where. All access is logged and subject to review.

#### **3\) Step-by-Step Procedures**

**Procedure: Access Records with Least Privilege**

1. Before opening any patient record, state task purpose in your own words (mental or verbal check): "I need to \[specific task\] for \[specific reason\]."  
2. Confirm patient identifier matches task (right patient, right account)  
3. Open only the minimum screens/sections required:  
   * For benefits verification: demographics and insurance section only  
   * For payment posting: billing/payment screens only  
   * For appointment scheduling: scheduling and contact information only  
   * Avoid opening clinical notes, test results, or diagnoses unless directly required for your specific task  
4. Complete task efficiently without browsing unrelated sections  
5. Log out or close record when task complete  
6. If you accidentally open wrong record or unnecessary section: close immediately, do not read content, document access in incident report if you viewed PHI

**Procedure: Recognize and Prevent Curiosity Browsing**

1. Apply curiosity self-check before accessing any record: "Do I have a job need to access this record right now?"  
2. High-risk scenarios requiring extra vigilance:  
   * Records of celebrities, public figures, or anyone in news  
   * Records of neighbors, friends, family members, coworkers, yourself  
   * Records tied to newsworthy events (crimes, accidents, unusual diagnoses)  
   * Records you're personally interested in for non-work reasons  
3. If you identify personal interest without job need: STOP, close without accessing, walk away  
4. If you already accessed without job need (even briefly): close immediately, report as incident to supervisor: "I accessed \[account\] without job need. I closed immediately. Please document this incident."  
5. Understand consequences: curiosity browsing typically results in termination under acceleration clause, even for first offense

**Safe Stop for Curiosity:** If you feel curious — stop and close. Curiosity is never a legitimate job need.

**Procedure: Request Access When Current Permissions Insufficient**

1. Identify specific access need: "I need \[specific permission\] to \[specific task\]."  
2. Confirm need is ongoing or temporary:  
   * Ongoing: role-based access request (permanent permission)  
   * Temporary: time-limited access request with end date  
3. Complete access request form with required fields:  
   * Your name and current role  
   * System/module needing access  
   * Specific permission level requested (read-only, read-write, etc.)  
   * Business justification (why needed for job duties)  
   * If temporary: requested end date  
   * Supervisor approval signature  
4. Submit to IT/access management per client procedure (needs\_review)  
5. Do not attempt to access before approval granted  
6. If approval delayed and task urgent: escalate to supervisor; do not improvise workarounds

**Procedure: Audit Your Own Access Permissions (Self-Audit Lab)**

1. Schedule self-audit quarterly or per client requirement (needs\_review)  
2. Access your user profile in each system you use  
3. Review current permissions list  
4. For each permission, ask: "Do I currently need this for my role?"  
5. Identify unused permissions:  
   * Permissions from prior role no longer applicable  
   * Temporary access granted for completed project  
   * System access for pods/clients no longer assigned to you  
6. Draft access removal request for identified unused permissions  
7. Submit to supervisor for approval and processing  
8. Document self-audit completion with date and findings

**Safe Stop Rules:**

* Stop if you feel curiosity about a record — close without accessing  
* Stop if you need access you don't have — request formally, do not borrow credentials  
* Stop if you realize you have permissions you don't use — request removal

**Escalation Path:** Supervisor → IT/access management → Compliance (for access violations)

**Documentation Requirements:**

* Access requests: system, permission, justification, approval, grant date  
* Access removals: system, permission removed, date  
* Self-audit: date conducted, permissions reviewed, removals requested  
* Curiosity browsing incidents: immediate self-report with account accessed and acknowledgment

#### **4\) Worked Examples**

**Compliant Example 1:** Employee's role is Order Entry Specialist. Receives access request form approval for new payer portal required for job. Form specifies: System: ABC Insurance Portal, Permission: Read claims status, Justification: "Required to verify prior authorization status for orders per role duties," Supervisor signature present, End date: None (ongoing role need). Employee submits to IT, waits for access grant notification, then uses portal for job tasks only.

**Why Compliant:** Formal access request with specific scope and justification, supervisor approved, waited for proper grant, uses for stated purpose only.

**Compliant Example 2:** Employee conducts quarterly self-audit. Discovers they still have "Pod 3" permissions from six months ago when temporarily helped that pod. Current assignment is Pod 7 only. Employee drafts access removal request: "Remove Pod 3 system access. No longer assigned to Pod 3 as of \[date\]. Current assignment is Pod 7." Submits to supervisor.

**Why Compliant:** Proactive identification of access creep, formal removal request, addresses unused permissions reducing risk.

**Non-Compliant Example (Source Scenario 20 \- Curiosity Browsing):** Employee lives in neighborhood. Neighbor was in car accident (employee saw ambulance yesterday). Employee is curious about neighbor's condition. Employee opens neighbor's chart during slow work period to "see diagnosis." Record shows multiple injuries and admission details. Employee reads for 2-3 minutes. System audit flags unauthorized access. Investigation confirms employee has no job need to access neighbor's record. Employee terminated under acceleration clause.

**Why Non-Compliant:** Textbook curiosity browsing violation. No job need, access driven purely by personal interest. Even brief duration and no sharing of information does not mitigate. Source explicitly identifies this pattern; Course 1 scenario library includes this example. Result: immediate termination, possible regulatory report depending on jurisdiction.

#### **5\) Practice Pack**

**Drill Set: Access Scenarios \- Allowed vs Violation (8 Scenarios)**

Classify each as: A) Allowed (legitimate access), V) Violation (unauthorized access)

1. Benefits coordinator accesses patient demographics and insurance screens to verify coverage → A  
2. Billing clerk opens clinical notes to "understand the case better" when processing claim → V (clinical notes not needed for billing unless specifically required for medical necessity determination per role)  
3. Scheduler accesses patient's full medical history to find appointment dates → V (appointment dates in scheduling module; medical history unnecessary)  
4. Claims specialist accesses colleague's spouse's account out of curiosity → V (curiosity browsing, no job need)  
5. Nurse coordinator accesses patient record to review medication list for care coordination call → A (legitimate treatment coordination task)  
6. Manager accesses multiple records to audit documentation quality per assigned audit project → A (legitimate operations purpose with defined scope)  
7. Employee accesses celebrity patient record to "see if they're really here" → V (curiosity browsing, no job need)  
8. Revenue cycle specialist accesses payment history for account they're assigned to work → A (legitimate payment task)

**Scoring:** 80% \= 7/8 correct (note: 6/8 is 75%, so must get 7/8)

**Scenario Exercise: Role Drift** **Scenario:** You were temporarily assigned to help Authorization Pod for two months. That project ended three months ago. You're now back to your regular Collections Pod role. You still have Authorization Pod system access (nobody removed it). Today, a colleague in Authorization Pod asks "Can you just check this authorization status quickly? You still have access."

**Question:** What should you do and why?

**Answer:**

1. Decline access: "I no longer have job need for that system since I'm not in Authorization Pod. I should not access it."  
2. Direct colleague: "Please route this through your pod supervisor or someone currently assigned to Authorization."  
3. File access removal request: Document that Authorization Pod access should be removed (access creep identified)  
4. If already accessed before realizing: Self-report as incident, document that temporary access wasn't removed after assignment ended

**Why Correct:** Access permissions should match current role. Helping another pod using old permissions violates least privilege even if you're capable of the task. Identifies access creep and takes corrective action.

**Mini-Simulation Script:** **Trainer plays:** Peer asking for access help  
 **Learner plays:** Employee applying access controls

**Scenario:** "Hey, I forgot my password and I'm slammed. Can I use your login really quick to check this one account?"

**Learner expected actions:**

1. Refuse immediately: "I can't share my credentials. That violates our access policy."  
2. Offer proper alternative: "Contact IT for a password reset. It usually takes 5-10 minutes."  
3. If peer pressures: "I understand you're busy, but sharing credentials creates security and compliance issues. I have to say no."  
4. Document pressure attempt if it continues (Source Scenario 13 addresses credential sharing pressure)  
5. Do NOT log in on peer's behalf or share credentials under any circumstance

**Checklist: Least Privilege Access**

* \[ \] Stated task purpose before opening record (mental check minimum)  
* \[ \] Opened only minimum screens needed for specific task  
* \[ \] Did not browse unrelated sections  
* \[ \] Did not access records of known individuals without job need  
* \[ \] Closed record immediately after task completion  
* \[ \] If curiosity arose: stopped and closed without accessing  
* \[ \] If access insufficient: filed formal access request, did not borrow credentials  
* \[ \] Conducted self-audit on schedule, identified unused permissions  
* \[ \] Documented any incidents (accidental access, curiosity stopped, etc.)

**Scoring Rubric:**

* Access scenario identification (8 items): 4 points (7/8 \= 87.5%)  
* Least privilege procedure: 2 points  
* Curiosity prevention: 2 points  
* Access request/removal understanding: 2 points  
* **Total: 10 points (8/10 \= 80% passing)**

#### **6\) Knowledge Check (15 Items)**

**Item 1 (MCQ):** Least privilege means: A) Minimum access needed for job duties  
 B) No access without supervisor present  
 C) Access only during business hours  
 D) Shared access across teams

**Answer:** A  
 **Rationale:** Least privilege \= minimum access needed for assigned duties.

**Item 2 (MCQ):** Curiosity browsing is: A) Reading medical journals  
 B) Accessing patient records without job need  
 C) Browsing training materials  
 D) Reviewing your own medical records

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Curiosity browsing \= accessing records without legitimate job need, driven by personal interest.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** D — accessing own records may be permitted depending on policy but is separate issue.

**Item 3 (True/False):** If you have system access permission, you can use it for any patient in that system.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Access permission does not equal need-to-know; must have job need for each specific access.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — conflating technical access with legitimate authorization.

**Item 4 (MCQ):** Access creep occurs when: A) System runs slowly  
 B) Permissions accumulate over time without removal  
 C) Too many people have access  
 D) Access requests take too long

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Access creep \= accumulation of unnecessary permissions over time.

**Item 5 (Scenario MCQ):** You need access to a new system for your job. Your colleague offers to let you use their login "until yours is set up." What do you do? A) Accept to avoid workflow delay  
 B) Decline and file formal access request  
 C) Use it but log with your name  
 D) Share your login in exchange

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Credential sharing prohibited; must use formal access request process.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — urgency does not excuse credential sharing (Source Scenario 13).

**Item 6 (MCQ):** Before opening any patient record, you should: A) Check if anyone is watching  
 B) State task purpose and confirm legitimate job need  
 C) Log the access manually  
 D) Ask supervisor permission

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Purpose check before access prevents unauthorized access.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** D — routine access within role doesn't require per-record supervisor approval.

**Item 7 (True/False):** Accessing a celebrity patient's record is acceptable if you only look briefly and don't tell anyone.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Any curiosity browsing is violation regardless of duration or disclosure.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — "brief" and "didn't share" do not mitigate unauthorized access.

**Item 8 (MCQ):** Self-audit of access permissions should occur: A) Never; IT manages access  
 B) Only when changing roles  
 C) Quarterly or per client requirement  
 D) Only if supervisor requests

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** Periodic self-audit (typically quarterly) identifies access creep.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — employees share responsibility for appropriate access.

**Item 9 (Scenario MCQ):** You accidentally open wrong patient record. What do you do? A) Close immediately and document if PHI viewed  
 B) Quickly fix your work in the wrong record  
 C) Delete the access log entry  
 D) Tell the patient you accessed their record

**Answer:** A  
 **Rationale:** Immediate closure minimizes exposure; documentation required if PHI viewed (incident reporting).  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — never delete or alter audit logs.

**Item 10 (MCQ):** Access request justification should include: A) Why it would be convenient  
 B) Specific job need and tasks requiring access  
 C) How long you've worked there  
 D) What other people have access

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Justification must demonstrate legitimate job need.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — convenience is insufficient justification.

**Item 11 (True/False):** If you discover you have permissions you no longer need, you can just not use them instead of requesting removal.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Unused permissions should be formally removed to reduce access creep and risk.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — "not using" doesn't eliminate the access risk; formal removal required.

**Item 12 (MCQ):** Accessing your neighbor's record to check on their health after you saw an ambulance is: A) Acceptable if you're concerned  
 B) Acceptable if you're a healthcare worker  
 C) Curiosity browsing violation, likely resulting in termination  
 D) Acceptable as long as you don't share information

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** Source Scenario 20 explicitly addresses this pattern; curiosity browsing with likely termination.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — concern does not create job need.

**Item 13 (Scenario MCQ):** Your role is Billing. You can see clinical notes in the system. Should you read them when processing claims? A) Yes, always for complete context  
 B) Only if needed for medical necessity determination per role procedures  
 C) No, never access clinical notes  
 D) Only if claim is complex

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Access only what's needed for specific task; clinical notes may or may not be necessary depending on billing task and role procedures.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — "complete context" violates minimum necessary.

**Item 14 (Short Answer):** What are the three types of high-risk curiosity browsing scenarios requiring extra vigilance?

**Answer:**

1. Records of celebrities, public figures, or people in news  
2. Records of people you know (neighbors, friends, family, coworkers, yourself)  
3. Records tied to newsworthy events or unusual cases you're personally interested in

**Rationale:** These scenarios create personal interest that conflicts with job need.

**Item 15 (MCQ):** Consequences of curiosity browsing typically include: A) Counseling for first offense  
 B) Written warning  
 C) Termination under acceleration clause, even first offense  
 D) Mandatory retraining

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** Curiosity browsing is high-severity violation typically resulting in immediate termination.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — curiosity browsing bypasses progressive discipline.

#### **7\) QA and Audit Hooks**

**Evidence of Competence:**

* Mock access request form completed with appropriate justification  
* Mock access removal request completed  
* Self-audit lab completion (documented permission review and findings)  
* Knowledge check score (15 items, 12/15 \= 80%)  
* Scenario drill score (8 items, 7/8 required)

**Supervisor Review Checklist:**

* \[ \] Learner understands least privilege principle  
* \[ \] Learner can identify curiosity browsing risks  
* \[ \] Learner knows how to request access formally  
* \[ \] Learner completed self-audit exercise appropriately  
* \[ \] Learner understands severity of curiosity browsing consequences

**Recordkeeping Tie-Back:**

* Module 3 completion with all artifacts logged  
* Access request/removal mock exercises scored and stored  
* Self-audit completion documented  
* Module 3 scores recorded as required training record fields

**\[CONTINUATION NOTICE: Completed Course 1 Modules 1-3. Continuing with Modules 4-16, then proceeding to Courses 2-6. Output continues...\]**

### **MODULE 4: PATIENT IDENTITY VERIFICATION AND CALLER AUTHENTICATION (45 MIN)**

#### **1\) Entry-Level Primer**

**Plain Language Summary:** Wrong-person disclosures are one of the most common PHI incidents. Verification prevents this: before disclosing PHI or granting access, confirm the requester is who they claim to be using approved factors (DOB, last-four SSN, verbal password, or other client-specific methods). Verification is scripted discipline — follow the script exactly, never skip steps even under pressure. If verification fails after three attempts, terminate interaction politely, do not hint at correct answers, document failure, and escalate.

**Observable Behaviors:**

* States purpose of verification at interaction start  
* Asks all required verification factors per client script  
* Does not hint at correct answers when factors mismatch  
* Terminates interaction after three failures per script  
* Documents verification outcome (pass/fail, factors used)  
* Never bypasses verification due to urgency or caller emotion

#### **2\) Definitions**

**Verification Factors:** Information elements used to confirm identity (DOB, last-four SSN, verbal password, account number with personal details, security questions). Client-specific factors need\_review per client policy.

**Wrong-Person Error:** Disclosing PHI to individual other than intended recipient due to identity verification failure.

**Refusal Script:** Pre-approved language for declining disclosure when verification fails, including explanation and alternatives offered.

**Three-Attempt Limit:** Standard maximum verification attempts before termination; prevents guessing attacks while allowing for honest errors.

**Verification Pass:** All required factors match records; interaction may proceed to disclosure decision (still requires authorization check per Module 2).

**Verification Fail:** One or more factors do not match; interaction terminates or limited alternatives offered (secure callback, written request).

**Verbal Password (needs\_review):** Optional patient-set password for phone verification; availability depends on client system capabilities.

**Security Questions (needs\_review):** Alternative verification method using pre-registered answers; client-specific availability and question sets need\_review.

#### **3\) Step-by-Step Procedures**

**Procedure: Verify Patient Identity (Phone or In-Person)**

1. State purpose of verification professionally: "To protect your privacy, I need to verify your identity before we discuss your information."

2. Request first verification factor per client script (needs\_review for specific factors and order):

   * Common first factor: Full legal name  
   * Confirm: "Can you state your full name as it appears in our records?"  
3. Request second verification factor:

   * Common second factor: Date of birth (format: MM/DD/YYYY)  
   * Confirm: "Can you provide your date of birth?"  
4. Request third verification factor if required by client:

   * Common third factor: Last four digits of SSN OR account number OR verbal password (if set)  
   * Confirm: "Can you provide the last four digits of your Social Security number?" OR relevant factor  
5. Compare provided factors against system records:

   * All factors match exactly: verification passes → proceed to Module 2 disclosure decision  
   * Any factor does not match: provide one additional attempt for that factor  
   * After THREE TOTAL FAILED ATTEMPTS across all factors: terminate interaction  
6. If verification fails:

   * Do NOT hint at correct answers ("Your birthday is in March, not April")  
   * Do NOT provide partial information ("You're close")  
   * Use refusal script: "I'm unable to verify your identity using the information provided. For your security, I cannot proceed with this request over the phone. You have these alternatives: \[list alternatives per script\]."  
7. Alternatives to offer after verification failure:

   * Secure callback: "I can call you back at the phone number we have on file after you confirm that number."  
   * Secure portal: "You can access this information through our secure patient portal."  
   * In-person with ID: "You can visit our office with photo identification."  
   * Written request: "You can submit a written request with your signature."  
8. Document verification outcome in system/ticket:

   * Pass: "Identity verified using \[factors\]. Proceeded with \[disclosure/action\]."  
   * Fail: "Identity verification failed after \[X\] attempts. Interaction terminated. Offered alternatives: \[list\]. No disclosure occurred."

**Procedure: Verify Provider/Payer Caller Identity**

1. Request caller identification:

   * Name and role/title  
   * Organization name  
   * Department  
   * Callback number (official organizational number, not personal cell)  
2. Verify identity using client-approved method (needs\_review per client):

   * Method A: Callback to verified organizational number from records  
   * Method B: Verify caller ID matches organizational number \+ verify personal details  
   * Method C: Use provider/payer directory to confirm person and role  
   * Method D: Request faxback on organizational letterhead  
3. If verification method is unclear or caller resists: escalate to supervisor before disclosing

4. Document provider/payer verification: "Verified caller identity: \[Name\], \[Role\], \[Organization\]. Method: \[callback/caller ID verification/directory confirmation\]."

**Procedure: Handle Verification Failures (Script Discipline)**

**Attempt 1 Failure:** "Let me try that information again. Can you provide your \[factor\]?" \[If fails again, this is Attempt 2\]

**Attempt 2 Failure:** "That information still doesn't match our records. I have one more attempt before I need to end this call for security reasons. Can you carefully provide your \[factor\]?" \[If fails again, this is Attempt 3 — terminate\]

**Attempt 3 Failure (Termination):** "I'm unable to verify your identity with the information provided. For your security, I cannot share any information at this time. You can \[list alternatives from script\]. Is there anything else I can help with that doesn't require identity verification?"

\[End call professionally. Do NOT continue discussing the account or providing hints.\]

**Safe Stop Rules:**

* Stop if caller refuses to provide verification factors — cannot proceed without verification  
* Stop if caller provides conflicting information ("My DOB is March... no, April...") — potential fraud indicator, escalate  
* Stop if caller pressures you to skip verification ("This is urgent\!" "I've called before\!" "Just this once\!") — apply Rule 4, maintain requirement  
* Stop if verification factors are not working and you suspect system error — escalate to verify correct factors before continuing attempts

**Escalation Path:** Supervisor → Privacy officer (for unusual verification issues or fraud concerns)

**Documentation Requirements:**

* Every verification attempt outcome documented (pass or fail)  
* Failed verification: number of attempts, alternatives offered, caller response  
* Verification factors used (record which factors, not the actual values provided)  
* Any fraud indicators or suspicious behavior

#### **4\) Worked Examples**

**Compliant Example 1 \- Clean Verification Pass:** **Caller:** "Hi, I need my test results." **Employee:** "I'd be happy to help. To protect your privacy, I need to verify your identity. Can you state your full name?" **Caller:** "Sarah Lee." **Employee:** "Thank you. Can you provide your date of birth?" **Caller:** "March 15, 1980." **Employee:** "And can you provide the last four digits of your Social Security number?" **Caller:** "5678." **Employee:** \[Checks system — all match\] "Thank you, I've verified your identity. Let me look up your test results." \[Proceeds with disclosure per Module 2\]

**Why Compliant:** Followed script exactly, verified all required factors before proceeding, documented verification pass.

**Compliant Example 2 \- Verification Failure with Proper Termination (Source Scenario 16):** **Caller:** "I need to schedule an appointment." **Employee:** "I can help with that. To access your account, I need to verify your identity. Can you state your full name?" **Caller:** "John Smith." **Employee:** "Thank you. Can you provide your date of birth?" **Caller:** "June 10, 1975." **Employee:** \[Checks system — DOB shows June 10, 1985\] "Let me try that date of birth again. Can you provide your date of birth?" **Caller:** "June 10, 1975." **Employee:** "That still doesn't match our records. I have one more attempt. Can you carefully provide your date of birth?" **Caller:** "I'm sure it's June 10, 1975." **Employee:** \[Three attempts exhausted\] "I'm unable to verify your identity with the information provided. For your security, I cannot access your account at this time. You can visit our office with photo identification, or you can call back with the correct information. Is there anything else I can help with?" **Caller:** "No." \[Call ends\] **Employee:** \[Documents: "Verification failed after 3 DOB attempts. Interaction terminated per script. No disclosure occurred."\]

**Why Compliant:** Followed three-attempt limit exactly, did not hint at correct answer (did not say "your birth year is 1985"), terminated per script, offered alternatives, documented completely, no disclosure occurred. Source Scenario 16 directly addresses this pattern.

**Non-Compliant Example:** **Caller:** "This is ridiculous\! I'm calling about my mother's surgery. It's scheduled for tomorrow and I need the time\!" **Employee:** "Okay, let me verify your identity first—" **Caller:** "I don't have time for this\! Just tell me the time\! She's 82 years old and we need to know\!" **Employee:** \[Feeling pressured\] "Alright, the surgery is at 7 AM. Make sure she's there by 6 AM for prep."

**Why Non-Compliant:** Multiple violations:

* Skipped identity verification entirely due to pressure (Rule 4 violation)  
* Did not verify caller is authorized to receive mother's information (Module 2 authorization requirement)  
* Disclosed surgery details to unverified, possibly unauthorized person  
* Wrong-person disclosure incident  
* Source patterns in Scenario 2 (family member without authorization) and Module 0 Rule 4 (pressure to bypass)  
* Result: incident report required, sanctions likely, potential breach if information went to wrong person

#### **5\) Practice Pack**

**Drill Set: Verification Script Repetition (10 Cooperative to Challenging Progression)**

Practice verification script with increasing difficulty (trainer plays caller, learner follows script):

**Round 1:** Cooperative caller, all factors match first attempt → PASS **Round 2:** Cooperative caller, DOB incorrect first attempt, correct second attempt → PASS **Round 3:** Caller provides incorrect DOB three times, polite → TERMINATE **Round 4:** Caller resistant to verification: "I've called before, you should know me\!" → Maintain script, proceed with verification or terminate **Round 5:** Caller provides conflicting information: "My DOB is... wait, let me check..." → Note inconsistency, proceed carefully or escalate if fraud indicator **Round 6:** Caller refuses to provide SSN: "I don't give that out." → Offer alternative factor or terminate if no alternative available **Round 7:** Caller pressures: "This is an emergency, skip the questions\!" → Apply Rule 4, maintain verification requirement **Round 8:** Wrong-number scenario: Caller verifies identity but asks about different patient → Terminate, explain cannot discuss other patients **Round 9:** Caller becomes angry during verification failure → Remain professional, maintain script, terminate at three attempts **Round 10:** Potential impersonator: Caller hesitates on factors, asks leading questions → Escalate for fraud concern

**Scoring:** 80% \= 8/10 rounds handled per script

**Scenario Exercise: Spouse Without Authorization (Source Scenario 2 Extension)** **Scenario:** Male caller: "This is Robert Chen. I need my wife Linda's mammogram results. We're married. Her DOB is May 5, 1978." You verify his identity as Robert Chen (DOB matches your system for an account under that name). He asks for Linda Chen's results.

**Question:** What is the verification issue and what do you do?

**Answer:** **Issue:** Robert verified his OWN identity, but he's asking for Linda's (someone else's) information. Verification of Robert's identity does not authorize disclosure of Linda's information. This requires authorization check per Module 2\.

**Response:** "Mr. Chen, I've verified your identity for your own account. However, to share Linda's information with you, I need authorization from Linda. Is Linda available to speak with me to authorize this disclosure, or she can complete an authorization form allowing us to share her information with you. I'm unable to share her results without her permission."

**If caller pressures:** "I understand you're married, but our policy requires each patient's explicit authorization before we share their information with others, including spouses. This protects everyone's privacy."

**Why Correct:** Distinguishes between verification (confirming who caller is) and authorization (permission to receive someone else's PHI). Source Scenario 2 directly addresses spouse request pattern.

**Mini-Simulation Script:** **Trainer plays:** Various callers  
 **Learner plays:** Employee applying verification script

**Simulation scored on:**

* Stating verification purpose at start  
* Following script exactly (no skipping factors)  
* Handling failures per three-attempt protocol  
* Not providing hints at correct answers  
* Terminating appropriately after three failures  
* Remaining professional under pressure  
* Documenting outcome

**Checklist: Verification Discipline**

* \[ \] Stated verification purpose before requesting factors  
* \[ \] Requested all required factors per client script  
* \[ \] Did not skip factors even if caller seemed legitimate  
* \[ \] Did not hint at correct answers when factors mismatched  
* \[ \] Followed three-attempt limit exactly  
* \[ \] Terminated interaction after third failure  
* \[ \] Offered appropriate alternatives after failure  
* \[ \] Remained professional during caller frustration or pressure  
* \[ \] Documented verification outcome completely  
* \[ \] Distinguished between identity verification and authorization to receive PHI

**Scoring Rubric:**

* Verification script execution: 4 points  
* Three-attempt protocol adherence: 2 points  
* Termination handling: 2 points  
* Documentation: 2 points  
* **Total: 10 points (8/10 \= 80% passing)**

#### **6\) Knowledge Check (12 Items)**

**Item 1 (MCQ):** Before disclosing PHI, you must verify: A) Caller's phone number only  
 B) Identity using approved factors  
 C) Caller's reason for calling  
 D) Supervisor approval

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Identity verification using approved factors is mandatory before PHI disclosure.

**Item 2 (MCQ):** How many verification attempts are typically allowed before termination? A) 1  
 B) 2  
 C) 3  
 D) Unlimited

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** Three-attempt limit is standard to balance security with honest error allowance.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** D — unlimited attempts create guessing attack vulnerability.

**Item 3 (True/False):** If a caller becomes angry during verification, you should skip the questions to de-escalate.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Verification is mandatory regardless of caller emotion; Rule 4 applies to pressure.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — prioritizing de-escalation over security.

**Item 4 (MCQ):** When a caller fails verification, you should: A) Give hints to help them remember  
 B) Provide the correct information so they can try again  
 C) Not provide hints; terminate after three attempts  
 D) Transfer to supervisor to verify

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** No hints; hints compromise security and help potential impersonators.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — "being helpful" compromises security.

**Item 5 (Scenario MCQ):** Caller provides correct name and DOB but wrong SSN last-four three times. What do you do? A) Proceed since 2 out of 3 factors matched  
 B) Terminate per script; offer alternatives  
 C) Ask for different SSN format  
 D) Override and proceed since caller seems legitimate

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** All required factors must match; three failures trigger termination.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — partial match is not sufficient.

**Item 6 (MCQ):** "To protect your privacy, I need to verify your identity" is: A) Optional preamble  
 B) Required statement of verification purpose  
 C) Only needed for new patients  
 D) Only needed for sensitive information

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Stating verification purpose is required at interaction start.

**Item 7 (True/False):** If you've spoken to the caller before and recognize their voice, you can skip verification.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Voice recognition is not approved verification method; script required every time.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — "I know them" is not verification.

**Item 8 (MCQ):** After verification failure, you should offer: A) One more chance with easier questions  
 B) Alternatives (secure callback, portal, in-person, written request)  
 C) To speak to supervisor who might recognize them  
 D) To call them back without verification

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Script specifies offering alternatives after verification failure.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — no "easier questions" exist.

**Item 9 (Scenario MCQ):** Caller says "I'm calling about my wife's appointment. Can you tell me the time?" What is the first verification step? A) Verify caller's identity  
 B) Verify wife's identity  
 C) Verify caller has authorization to receive wife's information  
 D) Just provide the appointment time

**Answer:** A and C (both needed)  
 **For MCQ format, best answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** Caller is requesting someone else's PHI; requires both identity verification (who is caller) and authorization check (is caller authorized to receive wife's PHI per Module 2). If forced to choose one answer, authorization check (C) is the critical first failure point since caller is not the patient.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A only — verifying caller's identity is necessary but not sufficient; authorization required.

**Item 10 (MCQ):** Verification factors typically include (client-specific): A) Favorite color and pet name  
 B) DOB, last-four SSN, account number  
 C) Email password  
 D) Credit card number

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** DOB, SSN last-four, account numbers are standard verification factors (needs\_review per client).  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — these are too easily guessed or known by others.

**Item 11 (True/False):** If verification fails and the caller says "Can you just tell me if I'm close?" you should provide guidance.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Never provide hints about correctness or proximity; compromises security.

**Item 12 (Short Answer):** After three verification failures, what are three alternatives you should offer?

**Answer:** (Any three of: secure callback to number on file, secure patient portal access, in-person visit with photo ID, written request with signature)

**Rationale:** Standard alternatives after verification failure per script.

#### **7\) QA and Audit Hooks**

**Evidence of Competence:**

* Verification simulation score sheet (10 rounds, 8/10 \= 80%)  
* Knowledge check score (12 items, 10/12 \= 80%)  
* Script adherence evaluation (scored by trainer during simulation)  
* Documentation completeness check

**Supervisor Review Checklist:**

* \[ \] Learner follows verification script exactly  
* \[ \] Learner handles failures per three-attempt protocol  
* \[ \] Learner does not provide hints  
* \[ \] Learner terminates appropriately after failures  
* \[ \] Learner remains professional under caller pressure  
* \[ \] Learner documents verification outcomes completely  
* \[ \] Learner distinguishes identity verification from authorization

**Recordkeeping Tie-Back:**

* Module 4 completion with simulation score logged  
* Verification script competency flag set in training record  
* Module 4 scores recorded as required training record fields
