### **MODULE 5: REMOTE WORKSTATION SECURITY (PAKISTAN OPERATIONS) (90 MIN)**

#### **1\) Entry-Level Primer**

**Plain Language Summary:** Remote work from home in Pakistan introduces physical security risks that office environments control automatically (locked doors, limited access, security guards). You are responsible for creating a secure workspace: screen angle and privacy filter preventing visual access, lock discipline (manual lock every absence, auto-lock enabled), no non-work persons in view/hearing of PHI, network discipline (only approved secure connections), and incident response (if workspace compromised, stop work, escalate, document). Home environment interruptions (family, visitors, power outages, shared devices) require specific protocols to maintain PHI security.

**Observable Behaviors:**

* Positions screen to prevent viewing from windows, doors, common areas  
* Uses privacy filter on screen during work hours  
* Locks workstation manually before every absence (bathroom, breaks, end of shift)  
* Confirms auto-lock setting enabled (typically 5-15 minutes idle, needs\_review per client)  
* Mutes and pauses work immediately if non-work person enters visual/audio range of PHI  
* Uses only approved network connection methods (never public Wi-Fi or unsecured networks)  
* Reports any workspace compromise immediately  
* Maintains clean desk at end of shift (no PHI visible)

#### **2\) Definitions**

**Clean Screen:** No PHI visible on display; achieved by locking workstation, closing applications, or positioning away from unauthorized viewers.

**Clean Desk:** No PHI visible on physical workspace (no printed documents, sticky notes, notebooks with PHI); achieved by securing all documents in locked storage or shredding at end of shift.

**Privacy Filter:** Physical screen overlay limiting viewing angle, making screen content visible only directly in front, not from sides or behind. (needs\_review for client requirement; some clients require, others recommend)

**Visitor Protocol:** Procedure for handling unexpected non-work persons entering workspace during PHI handling (family members, delivery persons, neighbors, maintenance).

**VPN Discipline (needs\_review):** Use of virtual private network to secure connection to client systems. Client-specific VPN requirements and connection procedures need\_review.

**Public Wi-Fi Prohibition:** Absolute ban on connecting to client systems via coffee shops, libraries, airports, or other public networks regardless of VPN availability. Includes mobile hotspots from public locations.

**Workspace Compromise:** Any event where unauthorized person may have viewed, heard, or accessed PHI (screen visible to visitor, document seen, conversation overheard, device accessed).

**Secure Connection Methods (needs\_review):** Client-approved network connections; typically: dedicated home internet with password-protected router \+ VPN (if required), OR client-provided dedicated connection device. Specific requirements need\_review per client.

**Auto-Lock Setting:** System configuration causing workstation to lock automatically after specified idle period (typically 5-15 minutes, needs\_review per client).

**Manual Lock:** User-initiated immediate workstation lock via keyboard shortcut (typically Windows+L or Ctrl+Alt+Del \> Lock) before any absence.

#### **3\) Step-by-Step Procedures**

**Procedure: Set Up Secure Remote Workspace (Initial Setup)**

1. **Select workspace location in home:**

   * Private room with door preferred (not open living area)  
   * Screen positioned facing wall (not facing window or door)  
   * Chair positioned to see door entry (aware of people entering)  
   * Away from windows where outsiders could view screen  
   * Separated from family common areas if possible  
2. **Physical security setup:**

   * Install privacy filter on screen per client requirement (needs\_review)  
   * Verify screen angle prevents viewing from doorway  
   * Remove or cover any reflective surfaces (mirrors, glass surfaces) that could reflect screen  
   * Ensure door can be closed during work hours  
   * If door cannot close: use room divider or screen to create visual barrier  
3. **Technical security setup:**

   * Verify network connection meets client requirements (needs\_review):  
     * Password-protected home router (change default router password)  
     * VPN installed and functioning if required  
     * Never use public Wi-Fi or neighbor's Wi-Fi  
   * Enable auto-lock setting per client requirement (verify time setting, typically 5-15 minutes)  
   * Set strong workstation password (per Module 6\)  
   * Test manual lock shortcut (Windows+L) for muscle memory  
4. **Document workspace setup:**

   * Take photos showing screen position, privacy filter, workspace boundaries  
   * Submit to supervisor for approval per onboarding requirement  
   * If workspace changes (moving to different room): repeat documentation

**Procedure: Daily Secure Workspace Discipline**

1. **Start of shift:**

   * Close and/or lock workspace door  
   * Notify household members you are starting work, interruptions should be minimized  
   * Position screen at secure angle  
   * Verify privacy filter in place (if required)  
   * Log in to systems  
2. **During work:**

   * Manual lock before EVERY absence (bathroom, kitchen, doorbell, phone call):  
     * Press Windows+L (or equivalent) BEFORE standing up  
     * Verify lock screen displayed  
     * Walk away  
   * If family member or visitor enters unexpectedly:  
     * Immediately mute audio if on call  
     * Press Windows+L to lock screen  
     * Pause work, address interruption  
     * Verify no PHI visible on screen after lock  
     * Resume only after interruption cleared  
   * If interruption cannot be cleared quickly (visitor staying, family needs assistance):  
     * Log out of systems if secure lock insufficient  
     * Notify supervisor of extended interruption if during active call/task  
     * Resume only when workspace secure again  
3. **Breaks and lunch:**

   * Manual lock BEFORE leaving workspace  
   * Close door if possible  
   * No PHI documents left visible on desk  
   * Verify screen locked before exiting room  
4. **End of shift:**

   * Log out of all systems  
   * Lock workstation  
   * Clean desk: all documents secured or shredded  
   * No sticky notes with PHI visible  
   * Close door to workspace  
   * Power down or lock computer per client requirement

**Procedure: Handle Workspace Compromise Incident**

**Compromise examples:**

* Family member saw screen before you locked  
* Visitor (delivery person, neighbor, maintenance) entered and may have viewed screen  
* Overheard PHI during your call (family member in adjacent room)  
* Screen visible through window to outside  
* Document left visible when someone entered

**Response steps:**

1. **Stop immediately:** If currently handling PHI, stop activity  
2. **Secure workspace:** Lock screen, secure any visible documents  
3. **Assess exposure:** What PHI may have been visible/audible? To whom?  
4. **Notify supervisor immediately:** Use secure communication channel (phone, secure message), describe what happened:  
   * "My \[family member/visitor\] entered workspace during \[call/screen display\] and may have seen \[patient account screen/document/heard conversation\]."  
   * Do NOT include patient identifiers in escalation message unless secure method  
5. **Document incident:**  
   * What happened, when, who may have seen/heard PHI, what PHI  
   * Immediate actions taken (locked screen, ended call, etc.)  
   * Follow Module 10 incident reporting procedure  
6. **Preserve evidence if needed:** Do not delete anything  
7. **Corrective actions:** Based on supervisor guidance, may include:  
   * Improved physical barriers (door lock, screen repositioning)  
   * Household re-education about workspace boundaries  
   * Workspace relocation  
   * Privacy filter installation if not already present

**Procedure: Handle Power Outages and Connection Issues**

1. **If power outage occurs during work:**

   * Systems will shut down (data loss depending on application)  
   * Notify supervisor as soon as possible after power restored  
   * Do NOT use public Wi-Fi or mobile hotspot from public location to reconnect  
   * Do NOT go to coffee shop or library to finish work  
   * Wait for home power restoration or follow client emergency work stoppage procedure  
2. **If internet connection fails:**

   * Troubleshoot per IT guidance (router restart, etc.)  
   * Notify supervisor of outage  
   * Do NOT use alternative unsecured connection  
   * Do NOT use neighbor's Wi-Fi or public hotspot  
3. **Document outage:**

   * Time of outage, duration, any work interruption impact

**Safe Stop Rules:**

* Stop if non-work person enters workspace while PHI visible — lock screen immediately  
* Stop if you cannot maintain workspace security (household emergency, construction workers entering home) — log out, notify supervisor  
* Stop if connection security is uncertain (network issues, VPN failure) — do not proceed until confirmed secure  
* Stop if privacy filter missing and client requires it — do not work until installed

**Escalation Path:** Supervisor → IT (for technical issues) / Compliance (for security incidents)

**Documentation Requirements:**

* Workspace setup photos (initial and any changes)  
* Workspace compromise incidents documented fully per Module 10  
* Daily clean desk/clean screen compliance (verified through periodic supervisor spot checks)

#### **4\) Worked Examples**

**Compliant Example 1 \- Interruption Handling (Source Scenario 4 \- Family Member Entry):** Medical scribe is documenting provider dictation via video call. Family member (spouse) unexpectedly enters room to ask a question. Scribe immediately:

1. Mutes audio  
2. Presses Windows+L to lock screen  
3. Turns to family member: "I'm on a work call. Can this wait five minutes?"  
4. Family member leaves  
5. Scribe verifies screen locked (PHI not visible), resumes call  
6. Documents in end-of-shift note: "Brief interruption, family member entered workspace, workspace secured immediately, no PHI exposure"

**Why Compliant:** Immediate containment actions (mute, lock), prioritized PHI security over social courtesy, verified security before resuming, documented incident. Source Scenario 4 directly addresses this pattern.

**Compliant Example 2 \- Power Outage Response (Source Scenario 15 Context):** Employee experiencing scheduled power outage in area (announced by utility company). Employee:

1. Notifies supervisor start of shift: "Power outage scheduled 2-4 PM today. I will be offline during that time."  
2. During outage: does not attempt to continue work via alternative methods  
3. After power restored: reconnects via secure home network \+ VPN, resumes work  
4. Documents: "Power outage 2:03-3:47 PM, offline during that time, resumed via secure connection after restoration"

**Why Compliant:** Proactive notification, did not use unsecured alternatives (no public Wi-Fi, no mobile hotspot from public location), documented outage. Source Scenario 15 addresses network security discipline.

**Non-Compliant Example:** Employee works in shared living space (no door). Family members frequently walk through area. Employee has call with patient discussing diagnosis. Family member overhears conversation while getting water from kitchen. Employee continues call without interruption. Family member later mentions to neighbor that employee was talking about "someone's cancer diagnosis."

**Why Non-Compliant:** Multiple violations:

* Inadequate workspace setup (no privacy from family)  
* Failed to stop when family member entered hearing range  
* PHI overheard by unauthorized person  
* No incident reporting after recognizing family heard PHI  
* Workspace compromise incident occurred  
* Source patterns in Module 5 workspace security principles  
* Result: incident report required, possible workspace prohibition (may be required to work from supervised facility if home workspace cannot be secured), sanctions

#### **5\) Practice Pack**

**Drill Set: Workspace Security Checklist (Daily Practice)**

Complete checklist daily for first 30 days, then weekly:

**Setup verification:**

* \[ \] Screen facing away from window/door  
* \[ \] Privacy filter in place (if required)  
* \[ \] Door closed or visual barrier in place  
* \[ \] No reflective surfaces showing screen  
* \[ \] Auto-lock setting verified enabled

**During work:**

* \[ \] Manual lock before every absence practiced (Windows+L muscle memory)  
* \[ \] Family/household notified of work hours and boundaries  
* \[ \] No non-work persons in visual range of screen  
* \[ \] Secure network connection verified (home network \+ VPN if required)

**End of shift:**

* \[ \] All systems logged out  
* \[ \] Clean desk (no PHI visible)  
* \[ \] Screen locked or powered off  
* \[ \] Door closed to workspace

**Scenario Exercise:** **Scenario:** You are on a call verifying benefits. Doorbell rings. Delivery person at door requires signature. What are your exact steps?

**Answer:**

1. Tell caller: "Please hold for one moment."  
2. Mute call immediately  
3. Press Windows+L to lock screen  
4. Verify lock screen displayed (no PHI visible)  
5. Go to door, handle delivery (keep it brief)  
6. Return to workspace, verify door closed  
7. Unlock screen (password entry)  
8. Unmute call  
9. Resume: "Thank you for holding. Where were we?"  
10. Document if any unusual delay: "Brief interruption for delivery, workspace secured per protocol"

**Why Correct:** Lock screen BEFORE absence, verify security, handle interruption briefly, resume through proper unlock.

**Mini-Simulation Script:** **Trainer plays:** Supervisor conducting workspace security audit (virtual)  
 **Learner plays:** Employee demonstrating workspace

**Trainer:** "I need to verify your workspace security setup. Can you show me your screen position relative to windows and doors?"

**Learner expected actions:**

* Use camera to show screen position (facing wall, not window)  
* Show privacy filter if required/installed  
* Explain household boundaries established  
* Demonstrate manual lock (Windows+L)  
* Show clean desk (no PHI visible)  
* Discuss typical interruption scenarios and handling

**Trainer evaluates:**

* Physical setup adequacy  
* Lock discipline understanding  
* Interruption handling awareness  
* Clean desk/screen compliance

**Checklist: Remote Workspace Security**

* \[ \] Workspace physically secure (screen position, privacy filter, door control)  
* \[ \] Lock discipline practiced (manual lock every absence, auto-lock enabled)  
* \[ \] Household boundaries established (family aware of work privacy needs)  
* \[ \] Network security verified (approved connection method, never public Wi-Fi)  
* \[ \] Interruption protocol understood (mute, lock, pause, resume)  
* \[ \] Incident response understood (stop, secure, escalate, document)  
* \[ \] Clean desk/screen protocol followed daily  
* \[ \] Workspace photos submitted and approved

**Scoring Rubric:**

* Physical setup: 3 points (screen position, privacy filter if required, door control)  
* Lock discipline: 2 points (manual and auto-lock)  
* Interruption handling: 2 points  
* Network security: 2 points  
* Incident response understanding: 1 point  
* **Total: 10 points (8/10 \= 80% passing)**

#### **6\) Knowledge Check (15 Items)**

**Item 1 (MCQ):** Before standing up from your workstation for any reason, you should: A) Tell someone where you're going  
 B) Lock workstation manually (Windows+L)  
 C) Turn off monitor  
 D) Log out of all systems

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Manual lock required before every absence, even brief ones.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — turning off monitor doesn't lock system; session remains active.

**Item 2 (MCQ):** Privacy filter: A) Is optional convenience feature  
 B) Limits screen viewing angle to prevent shoulder surfing  
 C) Only needed in office settings  
 D) Replaces need for screen lock

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Privacy filter limits side/behind viewing angle for physical security.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** D — privacy filter supplements, does not replace, lock discipline.

**Item 3 (True/False):** If a family member enters your workspace unexpectedly during a call, you should finish your sentence before securing the workspace.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Immediate containment required (mute, lock) when non-work person enters.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — prioritizing conversation flow over security.

**Item 4 (MCQ):** Public Wi-Fi use for client work is: A) Allowed if using VPN  
 B) Allowed if no PHI visible  
 C) Allowed in emergencies  
 D) Absolutely prohibited

**Answer:** D  
 **Rationale:** Public Wi-Fi prohibited regardless of VPN or content; security risk too high.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — VPN does not make public Wi-Fi acceptable.

**Item 5 (Scenario MCQ):** Power outage at your home during work hours. What do you do? A) Go to coffee shop to continue work  
 B) Use mobile hotspot from library  
 C) Notify supervisor, wait for home power restoration  
 D) Use neighbor's Wi-Fi temporarily

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** No alternative unsecured connections allowed; wait for secure home connection restoration.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — coffee shop Wi-Fi is public and prohibited.

**Item 6 (MCQ):** Clean desk at end of shift means: A) Tidy appearance  
 B) No PHI visible; all documents secured or shredded  
 C) Monitor off  
 D) Keyboard covered

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Clean desk \= no PHI visible on physical workspace.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — "tidy" is not the security requirement; PHI removal is.

**Item 7 (True/False):** Auto-lock setting eliminates the need for manual locking before absences.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Manual lock required before every absence; auto-lock is backup for forgotten manual locks.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — relying solely on auto-lock leaves exposure during absence before timer triggers.

**Item 8 (MCQ):** If you realize a family member saw your screen before you locked it, you should: A) Do nothing if they didn't say anything  
 B) Immediately report as workspace compromise incident  
 C) Only report if they saw sensitive diagnoses  
 D) Ask them not to tell anyone

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Any potential PHI exposure is reportable incident, regardless of content type or outcome.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — "didn't say anything" doesn't eliminate incident reporting requirement.

**Item 9 (Scenario MCQ):** Your screen position faces a window where neighbors could potentially see if looking. What do you do? A) Keep working; unlikely anyone is looking  
 B) Reposition screen immediately to face wall, not window  
 C) Close blinds and continue  
 D) Only worry about it during daytime

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Screen must be positioned to prevent any external viewing possibility; repositioning is required.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — blinds can open or be imperfect; positioning is primary control.

**Item 10 (MCQ):** Workspace compromise incident requires: A) Stop work, secure workspace, notify supervisor immediately, document  
 B) Continue working and mention at end of shift  
 C) Only report if patient complains  
 D) Report only if you're certain PHI was seen

**Answer:** A  
 **Rationale:** Immediate response required: stop, secure, escalate, document.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** D — report potential exposure, not just confirmed exposure.

**Item 11 (True/False):** If your home internet is down, you can use your phone's mobile hotspot to continue work.

**Answer:** False (needs clarification based on client policy)  
 **Rationale:** Generally prohibited; mobile hotspots may not meet security requirements (needs\_review per client). If client explicitly allows secure mobile hotspots, answer may differ, but default is prohibition of alternative connections.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — assuming mobile hotspot is acceptable alternative.

**Item 12 (MCQ):** Manual lock keyboard shortcut (Windows) is: A) Ctrl+Alt+Del  
 B) Windows+L  
 C) Alt+F4  
 D) Ctrl+L

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Windows+L is standard Windows lock shortcut (Ctrl+Alt+Del also accesses lock option but requires additional click).  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — Ctrl+Alt+Del brings up security options menu, not immediate lock.

**Item 13 (Scenario MCQ):** You work in a room with a door. Your spouse knocks and opens door during your call. What do you do FIRST? A) Tell spouse to wait  
 B) Mute call and lock screen (Windows+L) immediately  
 C) Cover screen with paper  
 D) End call

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Immediate containment (mute \+ lock) is first action, before addressing person.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — securing workspace takes priority over social response.

**Item 14 (Short Answer):** List three physical security elements required for secure home workspace setup.

**Answer:** (Any three of: screen positioned away from window/door, privacy filter installed if required, door that closes or visual barrier, no reflective surfaces showing screen, separation from family common areas)

**Rationale:** Physical controls prevent unauthorized viewing.

**Item 15 (MCQ):** Clean screen means: A) Dust-free monitor  
 B) No PHI visible on display (locked or closed)  
 C) Screen protector installed  
 D) Brightness adjusted

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Clean screen \= no PHI visible, achieved through locking or closing applications.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — physical cleanliness is not the security definition.

#### **7\) QA and Audit Hooks**

**Evidence of Competence:**

* Workspace photos submitted and approved by supervisor  
* Workspace self-audit checklist completed (initial and periodic)  
* Remediation plan if deficiencies identified in initial setup  
* Knowledge check score (15 items, 12/15 \= 80%)  
* Simulation/scenario scores

**Supervisor Review Checklist:**

* \[ \] Workspace photos show adequate physical security (screen position, privacy filter if required, door control)  
* \[ \] Learner demonstrates manual lock discipline  
* \[ \] Learner articulates interruption handling protocol  
* \[ \] Learner understands network security requirements (no public Wi-Fi)  
* \[ \] Learner understands workspace compromise incident reporting

**Recordkeeping Tie-Back:**

* Module 5 completion with workspace approval documented  
* Workspace photos stored in employee file  
* Any workspace compromise incidents documented separately per Module 10  
* Module 5 scores recorded as required training record fields

**\[CONTINUATION NOTICE: Completed Course 1 Modules 1-5. Continuing with Modules 6-16, then proceeding to Courses 2-6...\]**

### **MODULE 6: AUTHENTICATION, PASSWORDS, AND MFA (60 MIN)**

#### **1\) Entry-Level Primer**

**Plain Language Summary:** Authentication is how systems confirm you are who you claim to be. Strong authentication prevents account compromise: follow exact client IAM (Identity and Access Management) rules (needs\_review for password requirements, rotation cadence, and MFA methods per client), enroll MFA (Multi-Factor Authentication) in approved method with backup codes stored securely, never share credentials under any circumstance (includes during emergencies or to "help" colleagues), respect session timeouts (re-authenticate after timeout, never bypass), and report any suspected compromise immediately. Password rotation cadence and exact controls need\_review against current standard and client IAM because NIST 800-63B guidance has evolved.

**Observable Behaviors:**

* Creates passwords meeting client-specific complexity requirements  
* Enrolls MFA during onboarding and maintains enrollment  
* Never shares credentials (username, password, MFA codes) with anyone  
* Respects session timeouts; re-authenticates without complaint  
* Uses password manager only if client-approved (needs\_review)  
* Reports suspected account compromise immediately (unusual login alerts, forgotten logins, suspicious activity)

#### **2\) Definitions**

**Authentication:** Process of verifying user identity before granting system access.

**Credential:** Information used to authenticate (username \+ password, biometrics, security token, MFA codes).

**MFA (Multi-Factor Authentication):** Security method requiring two or more verification factors: something you know (password), something you have (phone, token, smart card), something you are (fingerprint, face recognition).

**Credential Sharing:** Providing your login credentials to another person or using another person's credentials. Absolutely prohibited; results in immediate termination under acceleration clause.

**Session Timeout:** Automatic logout after period of inactivity (typically 15-30 minutes, needs\_review per client). Requires re-authentication to resume work.

**Password Manager (needs\_review):** Software storing encrypted passwords; reduces need to memorize multiple complex passwords. Client approval required before use; some clients prohibit, others require specific approved tools.

**Password Complexity Requirements (needs\_review):** Client-specific rules for password creation (length, character types, prohibited patterns). Common requirements: 12+ characters, mix of uppercase/lowercase/numbers/symbols, no dictionary words, no personal information. Exact requirements need\_review per client IAM policy.

**Password Rotation/Expiration (needs\_review):** Frequency of required password changes. NIST 800-63B current guidance discourages mandatory periodic rotation without cause; however, client policies vary and may require rotation (30-90 days common historically). Exact client requirements need\_review.

**Backup Codes:** One-time use codes generated during MFA enrollment for emergency access if primary MFA method unavailable (phone lost, token broken). Must be stored securely (not in plain text on device).

**Account Compromise:** Unauthorized access to user account due to stolen credentials, phishing, malware, or credential sharing.

#### **3\) Step-by-Step Procedures**

**Procedure: Enroll MFA During Onboarding**

1. Receive MFA enrollment instructions from IT during onboarding

2. Identify client-approved MFA method (needs\_review per client; common methods: authenticator app, SMS codes, hardware token, biometric)

3. Follow enrollment steps for approved method:

    **For Authenticator App (e.g., Microsoft Authenticator, Google Authenticator):**

   * Install approved authenticator app on personal phone  
   * Scan QR code displayed during enrollment  
   * Enter verification code generated by app to confirm enrollment  
   * Note: phone must be accessible during every login  
4. **For SMS Codes:**

   * Provide verified phone number  
   * Receive test code via SMS  
   * Enter code to confirm enrollment  
   * Update phone number immediately if it changes  
5. **For Hardware Token:**

   * Receive physical token from IT  
   * Test token code entry during enrollment  
   * Store token securely; bring to work daily  
6. Generate and securely store backup codes:

   * System generates 10-12 backup codes during enrollment  
   * Print backup codes OR write down and store in secure location (NOT on device, NOT in email)  
   * Each backup code works once only  
   * Recommended: store in home safe or locked drawer, separate from device  
7. Verify MFA enrollment successful:

   * Log out completely  
   * Log back in using password \+ MFA method  
   * If successful: enrollment complete  
   * If unsuccessful: contact IT immediately for troubleshooting  
8. Document MFA enrollment completion in training record

**Procedure: Daily Authentication Discipline**

1. **Login at start of shift:**

   * Enter username  
   * Enter password (do not save password in browser unless client-approved password manager policy permits)  
   * Complete MFA prompt (enter code from app, SMS, or token)  
   * Verify successful login to all required systems  
2. **During work session:**

   * Do not walk away leaving session unlocked (manual lock per Module 5\)  
   * If session timeout occurs during work:  
     * Save work if possible before timeout  
     * Re-authenticate through login screen  
     * Resume work  
   * Do NOT attempt to bypass or extend session timeout  
   * Do NOT share screen while password visible during login  
3. **End of shift:**

   * Log out of all systems manually (do not rely on auto-logout)  
   * Confirm logout completed before closing applications

**Procedure: Handle Credential Sharing Requests (Always Refuse)**

**Scenario A: Colleague asks to borrow login** Response: "I cannot share my credentials. That violates our security policy and puts both of us at risk. If you need system access, please contact IT for your own credentials \[or password reset\]."

**Scenario B: Supervisor asks for password "just this once"** Response: "I need to follow our credential policy and cannot share my password. Can I help you with the task directly, or we can escalate to IT for proper access?" If supervisor insists: "I need to escalate this request to \[compliance/HR\] to confirm this is an approved exception." \[Then escalate immediately per safe stop Rule 4\]

**Scenario C: Colleague's account locked, asks to use yours to meet deadline** Response: "I can't share my credentials. Let me help you contact IT for an emergency unlock. Usually takes 10-15 minutes." Do NOT offer your login as temporary solution.

**Scenario D: IT or supervisor requests password over phone/email** Response: "Our policy is that I never provide my password to anyone, including IT or supervisors. Can you verify this is a legitimate request through \[proper IT ticket channel/official company directory\]?" Legitimate IT never asks for your password; if request persists, escalate to security/compliance.

**Procedure: Respond to Suspected Account Compromise**

**Warning signs of compromise:**

* Login alert from unfamiliar location/device  
* System shows active session you don't recognize  
* Password no longer works (changed without your action)  
* Unusual activity in your account history (emails sent, files accessed)  
* MFA codes requested that you didn't initiate

**Response steps:**

1. **Stop work immediately**  
2. **Change password immediately** if you can still log in:  
   * Use password reset process  
   * Create completely new password (do not reuse old password with minor changes)  
   * Verify MFA still enrolled correctly  
3. **If you cannot log in** (password changed by attacker):  
   * Contact IT immediately for emergency password reset  
   * Do NOT attempt multiple failed login attempts  
4. **Report to supervisor immediately:** "I suspect my account may be compromised. I've changed my password \[or cannot log in\]. Please log this as a security incident."  
5. **Document incident:**  
   * What you observed (login alert, failed password, unusual activity)  
   * When you first noticed  
   * Actions taken (password changed, IT contacted)  
   * Follow Module 10 incident reporting procedure  
6. **Cooperate with investigation:**  
   * IT may review login history  
   * May require additional authentication steps  
   * Do not delete any suspicious emails or activity evidence

**Safe Stop Rules:**

* Stop if anyone asks for your password — never provide, escalate  
* Stop if session timeout frustrates you — do not seek bypass; re-authenticate properly  
* Stop if MFA enrollment fails — contact IT before attempting workarounds  
* Stop if you suspect account compromise — change password, escalate immediately

**Escalation Path:** IT helpdesk → IT security → Compliance (for credential sharing or compromise incidents)

**Documentation Requirements:**

* MFA enrollment verification recorded in training record  
* Credential sharing refusals documented if pressure occurred (incident report per Module 10\)  
* Account compromise incidents documented fully (Module 10 procedure)

#### **4\) Worked Examples**

**Compliant Example 1 \- MFA Enrollment:** New hire receives enrollment email from IT: "Complete MFA enrollment by end of Day 1." Employee:

1. Installs Microsoft Authenticator app on personal phone  
2. Scans QR code from enrollment portal  
3. Tests code entry successfully  
4. Generates backup codes, prints them, stores in home desk drawer  
5. Logs out and back in successfully using password \+ MFA code  
6. Confirms to IT: "MFA enrollment complete"  
7. Training record updated with MFA enrollment verification timestamp

**Why Compliant:** Completed enrollment per instructions, stored backup codes securely (not on device), verified enrollment functionality, documented completion.

**Compliant Example 2 \- Credential Sharing Refusal (Source Scenario 13):** Colleague urgently requests: "My account is locked and I need to process this claim today. Can I use your login for 10 minutes? I'll log you out when done."

Employee response: "I can't share my credentials — that's against our policy and could get us both terminated. Let me help you contact IT for an emergency unlock. Usually takes about 15 minutes. Do you have IT's number?"

Colleague: "This is urgent\! I'll lose my bonus if I miss this deadline\!"

Employee: "I understand the urgency, but I still can't share my login. Let's get IT on the phone right now together." \[Helps colleague contact IT, documents pressure attempt in end-of-shift note\]

**Why Compliant:** Refused credential sharing despite urgency and peer pressure, offered proper alternative (IT unlock), remained helpful without violating policy, documented pressure attempt. Source Scenario 13 directly addresses credential sharing pressure pattern.

**Non-Compliant Example:** Supervisor to employee: "I need to review some accounts and my login isn't working. What's your password? I'll just use yours quickly."

Employee: "Okay, it's MyP@ssw0rd123. Log out when done."

Two days later: Audit discovers supervisor accessed accounts outside their authorization using employee's credentials. Investigation traces unauthorized access to employee's account. Both supervisor and employee terminated under acceleration clause (supervisor for unauthorized access \+ credential misuse; employee for credential sharing).

**Why Non-Compliant:** Credential sharing is absolute prohibition with immediate termination consequence, even when requested by supervisor. Employee should have refused and helped supervisor contact IT for proper access or password reset. Source Scenario 13 and Module 6 explicitly prohibit credential sharing under any circumstance. Result: termination for both parties, possible regulatory report, potential breach if access was inappropriate.

#### **5\) Practice Pack**

**Drill Set: MFA Enrollment Simulation (Hands-On)**

Using test system provided during training, complete MFA enrollment:

1. Access MFA enrollment portal with temporary credentials  
2. Choose authenticator app method  
3. Scan QR code with personal device (or test device if personal device unavailable)  
4. Enter verification code successfully  
5. Generate backup codes  
6. Store backup codes securely (demonstrate secure storage method to trainer)  
7. Log out and log back in using MFA  
8. If enrollment fails: troubleshoot with IT support simulation

**Scoring:** Must successfully complete all 8 steps to pass (100% required for security-critical task)

**Scenario Exercise: Credential Sharing Pressure**

For each scenario, provide exact response:

**Scenario 1:** "I'm working from home and forgot my password. Can you tell me yours so I can log in?" **Response:** "I cannot share my password. Contact IT at \[number\] for a password reset. It usually takes 5-10 minutes."

**Scenario 2:** "The client is on the phone waiting\! I need access NOW and IT is slow\! Just give me your login\!" **Response:** "I understand the urgency, but sharing credentials could get us both terminated. Let me help you escalate to IT for emergency support, or I can handle the client call if I have appropriate access."

**Scenario 3:** Supervisor: "This is a direct order. Give me your password." **Response:** "Our policy prohibits credential sharing even by supervisor direction. I need to escalate this request to \[compliance/HR\] to verify it's an approved exception." \[Then immediately escalate\]

**Scenario 4:** "We're a team\! We share everything. What's your password for the shared account?" **Response:** "Even for shared tasks, we each need our own credentials. Sharing credentials is prohibited and creates audit and security risks. Let's get you set up with your own access through IT."

**Mini-Simulation Script:** **Trainer plays:** Colleague pressuring for credentials  
 **Learner plays:** Employee refusing and offering alternatives

**Round 1 \- Subtle Pressure:** **Trainer:** "Hey, can you log in for me on that system? I'm having issues and I'm slammed."

**Learner expected actions:**

* Refuse: "I can't use my credentials for someone else. What system are you having trouble with?"  
* Diagnose: "Is it a password issue or access issue?"  
* Direct to proper solution: "Contact IT for password reset" OR "Request access through access request form"  
* Do NOT log in on their behalf

**Round 2 \- Urgent Pressure:** **Trainer:** "This is URGENT\! Just tell me your password quickly\!"

**Learner expected actions:**

* Maintain refusal: "I cannot share my password under any circumstances, including urgent ones."  
* Offer alternative: "Tell me what you need done. If I have access, I can do it. Or we can escalate to IT for emergency support."  
* If pressure continues: Document pressure attempt, escalate to supervisor

**Round 3 \- Supervisor Pressure:** **Trainer (as supervisor):** "I need your password to review some records. This is an audit requirement."

**Learner expected actions:**

* Politely refuse: "Our policy prohibits password sharing. Can I help you access the records directly, or we can contact IT to provision audit access?"  
* If supervisor insists: "I need to verify with \[compliance/HR\] that this is an approved exception to the credential policy before I can provide my password."  
* Escalate immediately after interaction to compliance/HR

**Checklist: Authentication Discipline**

* \[ \] MFA enrolled and verified functional  
* \[ \] Backup codes generated and stored securely (not on device)  
* \[ \] Password meets client complexity requirements (needs\_review)  
* \[ \] Never shared credentials with anyone  
* \[ \] Respects session timeouts; re-authenticates without bypassing  
* \[ \] Logs out at end of shift manually  
* \[ \] Knows how to respond to credential sharing requests (refuse, offer alternative)  
* \[ \] Knows warning signs of account compromise  
* \[ \] Knows how to report suspected compromise (immediate password change, report to IT and supervisor)

**Scoring Rubric:**

* MFA enrollment completion: 3 points (must complete successfully)  
* Credential sharing refusal: 3 points (must refuse in all scenarios)  
* Session timeout understanding: 2 points  
* Compromise response: 2 points  
* **Total: 10 points (8/10 \= 80% passing; note MFA enrollment is 100% required but contributes to score)**

#### **6\) Knowledge Check (15 Items)**

**Item 1 (MCQ):** MFA requires: A) Very strong password only  
 B) Two or more verification factors  
 C) Biometric scan only  
 D) Security question answer

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** MFA \= multiple factors (something you know \+ something you have \+ something you are).

**Item 2 (MCQ):** You should share your password: A) With supervisors when requested  
 B) With IT when they ask over phone  
 C) With trusted colleagues in emergencies  
 D) Never, under any circumstance

**Answer:** D  
 **Rationale:** Credential sharing absolutely prohibited; no exceptions.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — supervisor requests do not override prohibition.

**Item 3 (True/False):** If your colleague's account is locked and they need urgent access, you can let them use your login temporarily.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Credential sharing prohibited even in urgent situations; direct colleague to IT for unlock.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — urgency does not excuse credential sharing (Source Scenario 13).

**Item 4 (MCQ):** Backup codes should be stored: A) In email for easy access  
 B) On your phone in photos  
 C) Securely separate from device (home safe, locked drawer)  
 D) In cloud storage

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** Backup codes must be physically secure and separate from primary device.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** B — storing on phone defeats purpose if phone is lost/stolen.

**Item 5 (Scenario MCQ):** Your session times out during work. What do you do? A) Try to bypass timeout using browser back button  
 B) Re-authenticate through login screen and resume  
 C) Contact IT to extend timeout setting  
 D) Use colleague's session on their computer

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Respect timeout; re-authenticate properly and resume work.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — attempting bypass violates session management controls.

**Item 6 (MCQ):** Warning signs of account compromise include: A) Slow system performance  
 B) Login alert from unfamiliar location, password no longer works  
 C) Too many emails in inbox  
 D) Scheduled maintenance notice

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Unexpected login activity or password change indicates potential compromise.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — slow performance usually not compromise indicator.

**Item 7 (True/False):** Password managers are always allowed for storing work passwords.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Password manager use requires client approval (needs\_review per client); some prohibit, others require specific tools.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — assuming password managers are universally acceptable.

**Item 8 (MCQ):** If you suspect account compromise, you should: A) Wait to see if it happens again  
 B) Immediately change password, report to IT and supervisor  
 C) Just change password quietly  
 D) Monitor the situation for a few days

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Immediate response required: change password, escalate to IT and supervisor, document.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — reporting required, not just password change.

**Item 9 (Scenario MCQ):** Supervisor orders: "Give me your password. That's a direct order." What do you do? A) Comply since it's a direct order  
 B) Refuse and escalate to compliance/HR immediately  
 C) Give password but document it  
 D) Negotiate a compromise

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Credential sharing prohibited even by supervisor order; escalate immediately.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — orders do not override security policy.

**Item 10 (MCQ):** Session timeout exists to: A) Annoy users  
 B) Prevent unauthorized access to abandoned sessions  
 C) Save system resources  
 D) Force password changes

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Session timeout security control prevents access if user forgets to log out or lock.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — resource saving is minor benefit, not primary purpose.

**Item 11 (True/False):** Legitimate IT staff will sometimes ask for your password over phone or email to troubleshoot issues.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** IT never needs your password; they can reset it but should never ask for it.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — not understanding that IT has administrative access and doesn't need user passwords.

**Item 12 (MCQ):** After MFA enrollment, you should: A) Delete backup codes since you have the app  
 B) Share backup codes with supervisor for safety  
 C) Store backup codes securely separate from device  
 D) Email backup codes to yourself

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** Backup codes must be physically secure and separate from primary MFA device.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — backup codes needed if primary MFA method fails.

**Item 13 (Scenario MCQ):** You receive email: "Your password expires in 24 hours. Click here to reset." What do you do? A) Click link and reset password  
 B) Suspect phishing; do not click; verify through official IT channel or login portal directly  
 C) Reply asking if email is legitimate  
 D) Forward to colleague to check

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Password reset phishing common; verify through official channels, never click links in unexpected emails.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — clicking link without verification is phishing vulnerability.

**Item 14 (Short Answer):** What are the three types of authentication factors in MFA?

**Answer:**

1. Something you know (password, PIN)  
2. Something you have (phone, token, smart card)  
3. Something you are (fingerprint, face recognition, biometric)

**Rationale:** MFA combines multiple factor types for stronger security.

**Item 15 (MCQ):** Credential sharing typically results in: A) Verbal warning  
 B) Written warning  
 C) Immediate termination under acceleration clause  
 D) Mandatory retraining

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** Credential sharing is high-severity violation triggering immediate termination.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — not understanding severity of credential sharing.

#### **7\) QA and Audit Hooks**

**Evidence of Competence:**

* MFA enrollment verification record with timestamp  
* Acknowledgment of credential sharing prohibition signed  
* Simulation performance (credential sharing refusal scenarios)  
* Knowledge check score (15 items, 12/15 \= 80%)

**Supervisor Review Checklist:**

* \[ \] Learner completed MFA enrollment successfully  
* \[ \] Learner can demonstrate login with MFA  
* \[ \] Learner understands credential sharing prohibition and consequences  
* \[ \] Learner knows how to refuse credential sharing requests  
* \[ \] Learner understands session timeout compliance  
* \[ \] Learner knows account compromise warning signs and response

**Recordkeeping Tie-Back:**

* Module 6 completion with MFA enrollment verification timestamp logged as required field  
* Credential sharing prohibition acknowledgment timestamp logged  
* Module 6 scores recorded as required training record fields

---

### **MODULE 7: EMAIL, MESSAGING, AND COMMUNICATION HYGIENE (75 MIN)**

#### **1\) Entry-Level Primer**

**Plain Language Summary:** Most real PHI incidents are communication mistakes: wrong recipient (auto-complete error, reply-all), wrong channel (SMS, WhatsApp, personal email instead of approved channels), too much detail (diagnosis in subject line, full medical history in body when only appointment time needed). This module trains PHI-safe subject lines (use generic subjects from approved library, never put patient identifiers or diagnoses in subject), channel discipline (approved platforms only; SMS/WhatsApp/personal email/social media/unapproved chat banned for PHI), recipient verification (check To/CC/BCC, confirm thread history, verify email domain), and escalation when channel approval uncertain.

**Observable Behaviors:**

* Writes subject lines from approved generic library  
* Verifies recipient identity and email address before sending PHI  
* Checks entire thread history before forwarding or replying  
* Uses only approved channels for PHI communication  
* Escalates immediately if uncertain about channel approval  
* Applies minimum necessary in email body (limits PHI to what recipient needs)

#### **2\) Definitions**

**Approved Channels:** Client-approved communication platforms for PHI handling (typically: client email system, secure portal, approved encrypted email, secure fax to verified number, secure provider portal, approved internal messaging systems). Specific approved list needs\_review per client.

**Banned Channels:** Communication platforms prohibited for PHI regardless of encryption or convenience (source list): SMS/text messages, WhatsApp, Facebook Messenger, personal email (Gmail, Yahoo, Hotmail, etc.), Twitter/X, Instagram, LinkedIn messaging, Telegram, Signal, Slack (unless client explicitly approves for specific client), unapproved chat applications, personal devices without MDM (Mobile Device Management).

**Auto-Complete Risk:** Email client suggests recipient based on first few letters; risk of selecting wrong recipient with similar name or email address.

**Reply-All Risk:** Responding to group email distribution includes all recipients, potentially disclosing PHI to unintended recipients who don't need-to-know.

**Thread History:** Previous email messages included in forward or reply; may contain PHI from earlier in conversation even if current message is PHI-free.

**Subject Line Library:** Pre-approved generic subject lines that convey purpose without including PHI (examples: "Claim status update," "Benefits inquiry follow-up," "Authorization request," "Account update needed," "Question regarding process"). Stored as job aid for reference.

**Encryption (needs\_review):** Technology protecting email content in transit and at rest. Client-specific encryption requirements and methods need\_review (some clients require encrypted email for external recipients, automatic encryption within system, or manual encryption selection).

**Email Domain Verification:** Confirming recipient email address domain matches expected organization (e.g., @clientorg.com for client staff, @insurancecompany.com for payer).

#### **3\) Step-by-Step Procedures**

**Procedure: Compose PHI-Safe Email**

1. **Before starting email:**

   * Confirm purpose: what specific information does recipient need?  
   * Confirm recipient: who exactly needs this information and why?  
   * Confirm channel: is email approved for this recipient and content type? (needs\_review per client)  
2. **Subject line discipline:**

   * Access subject line library job aid (approved generic subjects)  
   * Select appropriate generic subject from library:  
     * "Claim follow-up" NOT "John Doe's cancer treatment claim"  
     * "Benefits question" NOT "Sarah Lee diabetes coverage"  
     * "Authorization update" NOT "MRI authorization for patient \#123456"  
     * "Account inquiry" NOT "Payment question for Mary Johnson DOB 3/15/80"  
   * If no appropriate generic subject exists: create one without PHI and submit to supervisor for approval to add to library  
   * NEVER include patient name, MRN, diagnosis, account details, or any identifier in subject line  
3. **Recipient verification checklist (critical step):**

   * Type first few letters of recipient name/email  
   * STOP and carefully review auto-complete suggestions  
   * Verify correct recipient selected (check full email address, not just name display)  
   * Verify email domain matches expected organization (e.g., @clientorg.com NOT @gmail.com)  
   * For external recipients: verify organization is appropriate recipient for this PHI  
   * Check To vs CC vs BCC:  
     * To: Primary recipients who need to act  
     * CC: Recipients who need awareness (verify each has need-to-know)  
     * BCC: Rarely used; verify policy allows BCC for intended purpose  
   * If reply-all button clicked: verify all recipients have need-to-know for your response content  
4. **Compose body with minimum necessary:**

   * Start with greeting  
   * State purpose clearly  
   * Include only minimum necessary PHI:  
     * Use account number or MRN (needed for record identification)  
     * Avoid patient name if identifier already provided  
     * Avoid diagnosis or clinical details unless essential for stated purpose  
     * Use generic descriptions: "patient contacted regarding outstanding balance" NOT "patient with diabetes called about unpaid insulin claim"  
   * Provide next steps or required actions  
   * Professional closing  
5. **Attachment discipline:**

   * Attach only files recipient needs (not entire folder)  
   * Verify attachments contain only minimum necessary PHI  
   * Check file names: avoid PHI in file name itself ("Account\_789\_statement.pdf" NOT "John\_Doe\_medical\_bill.pdf")  
   * If client requires: encrypt attachments or use password protection (needs\_review)  
6. **Thread history review (before forward or reply):**

   * Scroll down and read entire thread history  
   * Identify PHI in previous messages  
   * Assess: Does new recipient need to see that historical PHI?  
   * If no: copy only relevant text to new email (do not forward full thread)  
   * If yes and historical PHI is excessive: redact unnecessary PHI from thread before forwarding  
7. **Final verification before sending:**

   * Re-read recipient email addresses (all To/CC/BCC)  
   * Re-read subject line (PHI-free?)  
   * Re-read body (minimum necessary?)  
   * Verify attachments correct  
   * Click Send  
8. **If error realized after sending:**

   * Attempt recall if client system supports (Outlook recall feature)  
   * Immediately escalate to supervisor: "I sent email with PHI to wrong recipient. Attempting recall. Please log incident."  
   * Follow Module 10 incident response procedure  
   * Do NOT delete sent email (preserve evidence)

**Procedure: Select Approved Communication Channel**

**Decision tree for channel selection:**

1. **Identify recipient type:**

   * Internal colleague (client employee): Client email system typically approved  
   * External provider: Secure provider portal, encrypted email, or secure fax (needs\_review per client)  
   * Payer: Secure payer portal, encrypted email, or secure fax (needs\_review per client)  
   * Patient: Secure patient portal, encrypted email (if patient enrolled), phone for discussion (with verification), postal mail  
   * Other: Escalate for guidance  
2. **For each recipient type, verify channel approval:**

   * Check approved channel list for client (needs\_review job aid)  
   * If channel not on approved list: STOP, do not use, escalate for approval or alternative  
3. **Common scenarios:**

   * "Can I text patient appointment reminder?" → NO if text includes PHI; YES if generic reminder only ("You have appointment tomorrow at 2 PM" acceptable if no diagnosis/provider name; "Your diabetes appointment with Dr. Smith" is PHI)  
   * "Can I email provider using their Gmail?" → Generally NO; use official provider portal or secure email to provider's professional email  
   * "Can I use WhatsApp to send colleague a patient question?" → NO; use client email system or approved internal messaging  
   * "Can I discuss patient information on Slack?" → Only if Slack is client-approved platform for internal communication (needs\_review per client)  
4. **If uncertain about channel approval:**

   * STOP (safe stop Rule 2\)  
   * Escalate to supervisor: "I need to communicate \[type of information\] to \[recipient type\]. Is \[proposed channel\] approved?"  
   * Wait for confirmation before proceeding  
   * Do NOT assume channel is acceptable because it's convenient or commonly used

**Safe Stop Rules:**

* Stop if subject line contains patient identifiers or diagnosis — rewrite with generic subject  
* Stop if recipient auto-complete shows multiple similar options — carefully verify correct recipient  
* Stop if uncertain whether channel is approved — escalate before sending  
* Stop if you notice PHI in thread history before forwarding — redact or copy text to new email

**Escalation Path:** Supervisor → IT (for technical channel questions) / Privacy officer (for policy interpretation)

**Documentation Requirements:**

* PHI-containing emails automatically logged by system (audit trail)  
* Wrong recipient incidents documented immediately per Module 10  
* Channel approval questions documented in escalation ticket

#### **4\) Worked Examples**

**Compliant Example 1 \- PHI-Safe Email:** **To:** benefits.coordinator@clientorg.com \[verified correct recipient\]  
 **Subject:** Benefits verification request \[from approved library\]  
 **Body:** "Hi \[Name\],

Requesting benefits verification for account \#789456. Authorization needed for outpatient procedure scheduled 12/20. Please verify coverage and notify if pre-authorization required.

Let me know if you need additional information.

Thanks, \[Your Name\]"

**Why Compliant:** Generic subject (no PHI), verified recipient, minimum necessary body (account number only, no patient name or diagnosis), clear purpose, professional.

**Compliant Example 2 \- Email Rewrite Removing PHI:** **Original (non-compliant):** **Subject:** John Doe cancer treatment  
 **Body:** "John Doe, DOB 3/15/70, MRN 123456, diagnosed with stage 3 lung cancer, needs authorization for chemotherapy starting next week."

**Rewritten (compliant):** **Subject:** Authorization request  
 **Body:** "Authorization requested for account MRN 123456\. Treatment start date scheduled for next week. Please confirm authorization status."

**Why Improved:** Subject now generic, removed patient name (MRN sufficient), removed diagnosis details (not needed if payer has MRN for lookup), still provides necessary information (MRN, timeline).

**Non-Compliant Example (Source Scenario 7 and Related Patterns):** **Situation:** Employee intends to email benefits coordinator Sarah.Johnson@clientorg.com. Types "Sarah" in recipient field. Auto-complete suggests:

* Sarah.Johnson@clientorg.com (correct)  
* Sarah.Jones@clientorg.com (different person, similar name)  
* Sarah\_Johnson@gmail.com (personal email)

Employee quickly clicks first auto-complete suggestion without verifying. Accidentally selects Sarah.Jones@clientorg.com. Email contains patient account details (MRN, name, diagnosis, claim information). Sends email.

One hour later, Sarah Jones (wrong recipient) replies: "I think you meant to send this to Sarah Johnson, not me. I don't work in benefits."

**Why Non-Compliant:** Failed recipient verification step, disclosed PHI to wrong internal person (no need-to-know). Source Scenario 7 addresses misdirected email pattern.

**Immediate response:**

1. Reply to Sarah Jones: "Please delete that email and do not forward. I've escalated this incident to my supervisor."  
2. Escalate to supervisor immediately: "I sent PHI email to wrong Sarah. Sarah Jones received instead of Sarah Johnson. Wrong Sarah has no need-to-know. She has been asked to delete. Please log incident."  
3. Follow Module 10 incident procedure  
4. Document carefully: auto-complete error, immediate discovery, containment attempted, escalated

**Result:** Incident documented, training refresher likely required on recipient verification, potential counseling depending on history. Demonstrates importance of recipient verification checklist.

#### **5\) Practice Pack**

**Drill Set: Subject Line Rewrite (10 Unsafe to Safe)**

Rewrite each non-compliant subject line using approved generic alternatives:

1. "Sarah Lee mammogram results" → "Test results available"  
2. "John Doe diabetes claim denied" → "Claim status update"  
3. "Patient \#123 hypertension medication question" → "Medication inquiry"  
4. "Mary Johnson DOB 3/15/80 appointment" → "Appointment confirmation"  
5. "Cancer treatment authorization approved" → "Authorization update"  
6. "Bob Smith's stroke rehab claim" → "Claim follow-up"  
7. "Jane Wilson psychiatric evaluation results" → "Evaluation follow-up"  
8. "Account 789 \- patient has COVID" → "Account inquiry"  
9. "Emergency: patient suicide risk" → "Urgent case coordination" \[AND use phone, not email\]  
10. "Re: Linda Chen's pregnancy complications" → "Patient follow-up"

**Scoring:** 80% \= 8/10 rewrites correct (removes all PHI, maintains general purpose)

**Scenario Exercise: Thread History Exposure** **Scenario:** You receive email from provider asking about authorization status for account 789\. You reply with status. Before sending, you notice thread history includes 5 previous emails discussing patient's diagnosis, treatment plan, and personal family situation (prior medical history questions). Does new recipient (provider) need all that thread history?

**Question:** What do you do?

**Answer:**

1. Assess: Provider asking about authorization status (needs authorization answer only, not medical history or personal details from thread)  
2. Do NOT forward full thread with reply  
3. Create new email:  
   * To: \[Provider email, verified\]  
   * Subject: Authorization status \- Account 789  
   * Body: "Authorization approved for account 789\. Valid dates: \[dates\]. Please let me know if you need additional information."  
4. Send new email WITHOUT thread history  
5. If provider needs historical context: they can request specific information, which you provide on minimum necessary basis

**Why Correct:** Removes unnecessary PHI from thread history, provides only what provider needs for current inquiry (authorization status), applies minimum necessary to historical emails.

**Mini-Simulation Script:** **Trainer plays:** QA reviewer checking sent emails  
 **Learner plays:** Employee explaining email decisions

**Trainer:** "I'm reviewing your sent emails from last week. This email has subject 'Patient account question.' Can you walk me through your subject line choice?"

**Learner expected actions:**

* Explain: "I used generic subject from approved library because the original question involved patient account details. 'Patient account question' conveys purpose without including identifiers or diagnosis."  
* If trainer shows example with PHI in subject: Acknowledge error, explain correct approach (generic subject), commit to following subject line library

**Trainer:** "This email was sent to three recipients in CC field. How did you verify they all needed this information?"

**Learner expected actions:**

* Explain: "I verified each recipient's role: benefits coordinator needs claim info, supervisor needs status update for reporting, patient accounts needs billing context. All three have need-to-know for their specific tasks."  
* If trainer identifies recipient without need-to-know: Acknowledge error, explain should have used To for primary recipient only, CC only for those needing awareness with legitimate need-to-know

**Checklist: Email Communication Hygiene**

* \[ \] Channel confirmed approved for recipient and content type  
* \[ \] Subject line from approved library (no PHI in subject)  
* \[ \] Recipient email address verified carefully (not auto-complete error)  
* \[ \] Recipient domain verified (matches expected organization)  
* \[ \] To/CC/BCC checked (each recipient has need-to-know)  
* \[ \] Thread history reviewed before forward/reply  
* \[ \] Body contains minimum necessary PHI only  
* \[ \] Attachments contain minimum necessary PHI only  
* \[ \] File names do not contain PHI  
* \[ \] Final verification completed before clicking Send

**Scoring Rubric:**

* Subject line safety (10-item drill): 4 points (8/10 \= 80%)  
* Recipient verification procedure: 2 points  
* Channel selection: 2 points  
* Thread history handling: 2 points  
* **Total: 10 points (8/10 \= 80% passing)**

#### **6\) Knowledge Check (15 Items)**

**Item 1 (MCQ):** Which subject line is PHI-safe? A) "John Doe's test results"  
 B) "Claim status update"  
 C) "Patient \#123 diabetes medication"  
 D) "Sarah Lee MRN 456789"

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Generic subject without identifiers or diagnosis.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — includes diagnosis.

**Item 2 (MCQ):** Banned channels for PHI include: A) Client email system  
 B) SMS, WhatsApp, personal email  
 C) Secure patient portal  
 D) Encrypted email

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** SMS, WhatsApp, personal email prohibited for PHI per source banned list.

**Item 3 (True/False):** If WhatsApp is encrypted, it's acceptable to send patient information via WhatsApp.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** WhatsApp is banned channel regardless of encryption.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — assuming encryption makes channel acceptable.

**Item 4 (MCQ):** Before clicking Send on email with PHI, you must: A) Just click Send quickly  
 B) Verify recipient email address, subject PHI-free, body minimum necessary, attachments correct  
 C) Only check subject line  
 D) Get supervisor approval

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Final verification checklist required before sending.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — "quickly" bypasses verification.

**Item 5 (Scenario MCQ):** Auto-complete suggests two Sarah Johnsons with similar emails. What do you do? A) Pick the first one  
 B) Carefully verify full email address matches intended recipient  
 C) Send to both to be safe  
 D) Use different communication method

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Careful verification prevents auto-complete errors.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — picking first without verification is common error pattern (Source Scenario 7).

**Item 6 (MCQ):** Thread history should be reviewed before: A) Every email  
 B) Only forward or reply  
 C) Only external emails  
 D) Never necessary

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Thread history may contain PHI that new recipient doesn't need; review before forwarding/replying.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** D — thread history exposure is common incident cause.

**Item 7 (True/False):** You can put patient diagnosis in email subject line if the email is only going to internal staff.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Subject line should never contain PHI regardless of recipient; maintains consistency and prevents accidental external sends.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — "internal only" does not excuse PHI in subject.

**Item 8 (MCQ):** If you realize you sent email to wrong recipient after clicking Send, you should: A) Delete the sent email  
 B) Attempt recall, immediately escalate to supervisor, follow Module 10 incident procedure  
 C) Send follow-up asking recipient to ignore  
 D) Do nothing and hope they don't read it

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Immediate incident response: recall attempt, escalation, documentation, evidence preservation.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — deleting sent email destroys evidence.

**Item 9 (Scenario MCQ):** Patient texts you: "What were my test results?" You should: A) Text back the results  
 B) Text back: "Results are normal"  
 C) Not respond via text; call patient or direct to secure portal  
 D) Text back: "Call us"

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** SMS prohibited for PHI; use approved channel (phone with verification or secure portal).  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** B — even "normal" is PHI (health information); text remains inappropriate channel.

**Item 10 (MCQ):** Approved subject line library exists to: A) Save time  
 B) Provide PHI-safe generic subjects to prevent identifiers in subject lines  
 C) Standardize formatting  
 D) Satisfy IT requirements

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Library provides pre-approved PHI-free subjects to prevent identifier exposure.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — purpose is security, not just efficiency.

**Item 11 (True/False):** If you're uncertain whether a communication channel is approved for PHI, you should try it once to test and see if it gets flagged.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Safe stop Rule 2: stop if channel approval uncertain, escalate before using.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — "testing" creates potential incident.

**Item 12 (MCQ):** Minimum necessary in email body means: A) Use smallest font  
 B) Include only PHI recipient needs for stated purpose  
 C) Never use patient names  
 D) Keep emails under 100 words

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Minimum necessary \= only what's needed for recipient's purpose.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — patient names may be necessary depending on purpose and recipient.

**Item 13 (Scenario MCQ):** You forward email thread to colleague. Thread contains 10 previous emails discussing patient's medical history. Colleague only needs current status. What should you have done? A) Forward full thread for complete context  
 B) Copy only relevant current status to new email, exclude historical PHI  
 C) Summarize entire thread in new email  
 D) Forward and mark as confidential

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Minimum necessary: provide only current status needed, not full historical thread.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — "complete context" violates minimum necessary if not all needed.

**Item 14 (Short Answer):** List three banned channels for PHI.

**Answer:** (Any three from source list: SMS/text, WhatsApp, personal email Gmail/Yahoo/Hotmail, Facebook Messenger, Twitter/X, Instagram, LinkedIn messaging, Telegram, Signal, unapproved Slack, unapproved chat apps)

**Rationale:** Source provides explicit banned channel list.

**Item 15 (MCQ):** Reply-all button should: A) Always be used for transparency  
 B) Be used carefully after verifying all recipients need your response content  
 C) Never be used  
 D) Only be used for urgent messages

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Reply-all requires verification that all recipients have need-to-know for your content.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — "transparency" doesn't override need-to-know requirement.

#### **7\) QA and Audit Hooks**

**Evidence of Competence:**

* Email rewrite set (10 subject lines, 8/10 \= 80%)  
* Channel selection quiz (scenarios with correct channel identification)  
* Thread history scenario response (scored on minimum necessary application)  
* Knowledge check score (15 items, 12/15 \= 80%)

**Supervisor Review Checklist:**

* \[ \] Learner uses subject line library consistently  
* \[ \] Learner sent emails show proper recipient verification (no auto-complete errors in sample)  
* \[ \] Learner applies minimum necessary in email body  
* \[ \] Learner uses only approved channels (random audit shows no banned channel usage)  
* \[ \] Learner escalates channel approval questions appropriately

**Recordkeeping Tie-Back:**

* Module 7 completion with all exercises scored and logged  
* Email rewrite set stored as evidence artifact  
* Module 7 scores recorded as required training record fields
