### **MODULE 8: PHISHING, SOCIAL ENGINEERING, AND MALWARE DEFENSE (60 MIN)**

#### **1\) Entry-Level Primer**

**Plain Language Summary:** Remote work increases exposure to targeted scams: phishing emails trick you into clicking malicious links or providing credentials, social engineering manipulates you into bypassing controls, CEO fraud uses urgency and authority to pressure inappropriate actions. Defense requires systematic inspection (sender exact-match check, domain look-alike detection, link hover verification, attachment caution, urgency language recognition) and immediate reporting via established procedure (never forward suspicious items to colleagues "to ask if safe"). Most successful attacks exploit human trust and urgency rather than technical vulnerabilities.

**Observable Behaviors:**

* Inspects every email using 12-point checklist before clicking links or opening attachments  
* Hovers over links to preview actual destination URL before clicking  
* Verifies sender domain exactly matches expected organization  
* Recognizes urgency/threat language as manipulation tactic  
* Reports suspicious items via official reporting channel immediately  
* Never clicks "just to see" or forwards suspicious items to colleagues  
* Applies phone-based social engineering verification (uses approved verification method from Module 4, never discloses PHI to unverified caller)

#### **2\) Definitions**

**Phishing:** Deceptive electronic message (email, text, chat) designed to steal credentials, install malware, or trick recipient into inappropriate actions. Common tactics: spoofed sender, urgent tone, requests for credentials/financial info, malicious links/attachments.

**Spear Phishing:** Targeted phishing attack customized for specific individual or organization using researched details (your name, role, recent projects, colleagues' names) to appear more legitimate.

**Social Engineering:** Psychological manipulation to bypass security controls; exploits trust, authority, urgency, helpfulness, or fear. Occurs via phone, email, in-person, or combination. Examples: impersonating IT requesting password, fake "supervisor" demanding urgent access, "vendor" requesting confidential information.

**CEO Fraud (Business Email Compromise):** Phishing pattern impersonating executive or authority figure making urgent request (wire transfer, credential disclosure, policy exception). Source identifies this as common pattern in healthcare remote work environments.

**Look-Alike Domain:** Domain name similar to legitimate organization but with subtle differences designed to deceive (examples: microsof**t**.com vs microsof**1**.com; clientname.com vs clientname-secure.com vs client-name.com). One character difference or added words/hyphens.

**Link Hover:** Previewing actual link destination URL by hovering mouse over hyperlink without clicking; reveals whether displayed text matches actual destination.

**Malware:** Malicious software (viruses, ransomware, spyware, trojans) designed to damage systems, steal data, or enable unauthorized access. Often delivered via email attachments or malicious links.

**Attachment Caution:** Scrutiny of unexpected attachments, especially executable files (.exe, .bat, .scr), Office documents with macros (.docm, .xlsm), compressed files (.zip, .rar), and files from unknown senders.

**Urgency/Threat Language:** Manipulation tactic using time pressure or fear ("Account will close in 24 hours\!" "Click immediately or lose access\!" "Verify now to avoid suspension\!"). Designed to bypass rational evaluation and trigger impulsive action.

#### **3\) Step-by-Step Procedures**

**Procedure: Email Inspection Routine (12-Point Checklist \- Job Aid)**

Perform this inspection for EVERY email before clicking links, opening attachments, or responding with sensitive information:

**1\. Sender Address Exact-Match Check:**

* Click on sender name to reveal full email address  
* Verify domain EXACTLY matches expected organization  
* Example: legitimate \= support@microsoft.com; phishing \= support@micros0ft.com (zero instead of O)  
* If sender domain unexpected or unfamiliar: STOP, proceed to point 2

**2\. Domain Look-Alike Check:**

* Compare sender domain character-by-character with known legitimate domain  
* Watch for: single character substitutions (rn appearing as m, 0 vs O, 1 vs l), added words (microsoft-support.com), added hyphens (micro-soft.com), different TLDs (microsoft.net vs microsoft.com)  
* If ANY doubt about domain authenticity: STOP, report as suspicious

**3\. Greeting and Personalization:**

* Legitimate business emails typically use your name  
* Generic greetings ("Dear Customer," "Dear User," "Hello,") may indicate mass phishing  
* Lack of personalization alone insufficient to determine phishing, but increases suspicion

**4\. Subject Line and Body Tone:**

* Urgent language: "Immediate action required\!" "Your account will be suspended\!"  
* Threat language: "You will lose access," "Final notice," "Legal action"  
* Too-good-to-be-true: "You've won," "Unclaimed refund," "Free gift"  
* If tone creates pressure to act quickly: STOP, slow down, apply full checklist

**5\. Spelling and Grammar:**

* Professional organizations proofread; numerous errors suggest phishing  
* Watch for: obvious typos, awkward phrasing, inconsistent formatting  
* Note: some sophisticated phishing is well-written; absence of errors doesn't guarantee legitimacy

**6\. Link Hover and Destination Check:**

* Hover mouse over ANY link without clicking  
* Read actual destination URL in browser status bar or tooltip  
* Verify destination domain matches displayed text and expected organization  
* Example: Link text says "microsoft.com" but hover shows "malicioussite.ru" \= PHISHING  
* If link destination doesn't match expectation or text: DO NOT CLICK

**7\. Attachment Caution:**

* Were you expecting this attachment?  
* Do you know the sender personally and did they tell you they were sending this?  
* File type: Executables (.exe, .bat, .scr, .com), macro-enabled Office docs (.docm, .xlsm), compressed files (.zip, .rar, .7z) are high-risk  
* File name: Does file name make sense for the purported purpose?  
* If unexpected attachment from unknown or suspicious sender: DO NOT OPEN, report

**8\. Requests for Credentials or Sensitive Information:**

* Legitimate IT never asks for passwords via email  
* Legitimate organizations never request SSN, credit card, account credentials via unsolicited email  
* Any email requesting login credentials, password, financial information, or personal data: STOP, verify through separate trusted channel (call official number, not number in email)

**9\. Sense of Urgency or Pressure:**

* "Act now\!" "Within 24 hours\!" "Immediately\!" "Before it's too late\!"  
* Urgency is manipulation tactic designed to prevent careful evaluation  
* If feeling rushed: STOP, slow down, apply all checklist points, consult supervisor if needed

**10\. Sender Legitimacy Verification:**

* Can you verify sender through separate channel?  
* For internal colleagues: confirm via phone, secure chat, or in-person  
* For external organizations: call official published number (not number in suspicious email), ask if they sent communication  
* If cannot verify: treat as suspicious

**11\. Context and Expectation:**

* Does this email make sense in context of your work?  
* Were you expecting communication from this sender about this topic?  
* Does request align with sender's normal role and communication patterns?  
* If out-of-context or unexpected: increase suspicion level

**12\. "Too Good to Refuse" or "Too Bad to Ignore":**

* Offers that seem extraordinarily beneficial (free money, prizes, unusual benefits)  
* Threats that seem catastrophic (account closure, legal action, data loss)  
* Both extremes are manipulation tactics  
* If email is at either extreme: HIGH suspicion, report

**Inspection Decision After Checklist:**

* **SAFE:** All checklist points pass, sender verified, context appropriate → Proceed with caution  
* **SUSPICIOUS:** One or more red flags → DO NOT CLICK/OPEN; report immediately  
* **UNCERTAIN:** Cannot definitively determine → Report to IT/security for evaluation

**Procedure: Report Suspicious Items (Never Forward to Colleagues)**

1. **Do NOT:**

   * Click any links in suspicious email to "see where it goes"  
   * Open any attachments to "check if it's safe"  
   * Forward to colleagues asking "Is this legitimate?" (spreads phishing to more people)  
   * Reply to sender asking if email is legitimate (confirms active email address to attacker)  
   * Delete immediately without reporting (prevents security team analysis)  
2. **Do:**

   * Use official reporting method per client (needs\_review):  
     * Method A: Forward to dedicated phishing report email (e.g., phishing@clientorg.com)  
     * Method B: Use "Report Phishing" button in email client if available  
     * Method C: Create IT security ticket with email forwarded as attachment  
   * Include in report: full email with headers (sender, date, subject)  
   * Notify supervisor: "I received suspicious email and reported via \[method\]. I did not click anything."  
   * If you already clicked before recognizing: Report immediately as potential compromise per Module 6  
3. **After reporting:**

   * Delete suspicious email from inbox after confirmation IT received report  
   * Watch for similar attempts (attackers often send waves)  
   * If colleagues ask about similar email: direct them to report via official channel, do NOT forward your copy

**Procedure: Phone-Based Social Engineering Defense**

**Common phone social engineering scenarios:**

* "IT helpdesk" requesting password for "system upgrade"  
* "Supervisor" requesting urgent information transfer  
* "Vendor" requesting confidential contract or patient information  
* "Colleague" from different location asking for credentials or system access

**Defense steps:**

1. **Apply verification from Module 4:**

   * Do not provide information or credentials based on caller claim alone  
   * Request callback number and verify through official directory  
   * If "IT": hang up, call official IT helpdesk number to verify request  
   * If "supervisor": hang up, call supervisor's known number to verify  
   * If "vendor": verify through contract or vendor registry, return call to known official number  
2. **Never provide credentials over phone:**

   * Legitimate IT never needs your password  
   * If caller requests password: refuse, report as social engineering attempt  
3. **Recognize pressure tactics:**

   * Urgency: "We need this now or systems will fail\!"  
   * Authority: "This is \[executive name\], I'm ordering you\!"  
   * Intimidation: "You'll be fired if you don't comply\!"  
   * Helpfulness exploitation: "I'm in crisis, please help\!"  
   * If experiencing pressure: STOP, apply safe stop Rule 4, verify independently  
4. **Document and report:**

   * After suspicious call: document caller details (claimed name/role/organization, callback number provided, request made)  
   * Report to IT security and supervisor  
   * "I received call from person claiming to be \[X\] requesting \[Y\]. I refused and am reporting for awareness."

**Safe Stop Rules:**

* Stop if email sender domain looks even slightly unfamiliar — verify before proceeding  
* Stop if email requests credentials or urgent action — verify through separate channel  
* Stop if feeling rushed to click/respond — slow down, apply full checklist  
* Stop if caller requests password or PHI without proper verification — hang up, verify independently

**Escalation Path:** IT security (for suspicious emails/calls) → Supervisor (for awareness) → Compliance (if credentials disclosed or link clicked)

**Documentation Requirements:**

* Suspicious email reports documented automatically when reported to IT  
* Social engineering call attempts documented in IT security ticket  
* If compromised (clicked link, opened attachment, disclosed credentials): Module 6 compromise response \+ Module 10 incident response

#### **4\) Worked Examples**

**Compliant Example 1 \- Phishing Detection (Source Scenario 3 \- Look-Alike Domain):** Employee receives email: **From:** support@micros0ft.com (note: zero instead of O in microsoft) **Subject:** Urgent: Verify Your Account **Body:** "Your Microsoft Office 365 account requires immediate verification. Click here to verify within 24 hours or account will be suspended. \[Link\]"

Employee applies checklist:

1. Sender address check: reveals micros0ft.com (zero instead of O) — RED FLAG  
2. Domain look-alike: legitimate is microsoft.com, this is micros0ft.com — PHISHING  
3. Urgency language: "immediate," "24 hours," "suspended" — manipulation tactics  
4. Does not click link or reply  
5. Reports via official channel: forwards to phishing@clientorg.com with note "Suspected phishing \- look-alike domain"  
6. Deletes email after IT confirmation  
7. Notifies supervisor: "Received and reported phishing attempt"

**Why Compliant:** Systematic checklist application, detected look-alike domain, did not click despite urgency pressure, reported properly, did not forward to colleagues. Source Scenario 3 directly addresses look-alike domain pattern.

**Compliant Example 2 \- CEO Fraud Recognition:** Email appears to be from CEO: **From:** Display name shows "CEO Name" but actual address is ceo@clientname-secure.com (note: legitimate is clientname.com, this adds "-secure") **Subject:** URGENT: Wire Transfer Needed **Body:** "I'm in a meeting and need you to wire $50,000 to this vendor immediately for critical contract. I'll explain later. Here are wire details..."

Employee recognizes:

* CEO fraud pattern (authority \+ urgency)  
* Domain is not exact match (clientname-secure.com vs clientname.com)  
* Request outside normal workflow (direct wire request to non-finance employee)  
* "I'll explain later" avoids scrutiny

Employee response:

* Does NOT proceed with wire transfer  
* Calls CEO directly at known office number: "I received email requesting urgent wire transfer. Can you confirm?"  
* CEO: "I didn't send that. That's a scam. Report it."  
* Employee reports to IT security as CEO fraud attempt  
* Documents and notifies supervisor

**Why Compliant:** Recognized authority manipulation, verified domain, independently confirmed with CEO through trusted channel, did not comply with fraudulent request, reported properly.

**Non-Compliant Example:** Employee receives email from familiar colleague's address with unusual request: "Hey, I'm working from home and my system isn't working. Can you send me the patient database export file? I need it for urgent report. Attach to reply. Thanks\!"

Email actually from legitimate colleague's compromised account (attacker has access).

Employee thinks: "This is from my colleague Sarah, her email address is correct. She must really need help."

Employee attaches database export file (contains PHI) and replies with file.

**Why Non-Compliant:** Multiple failures:

* Did not verify unusual request through separate channel (should have called Sarah to confirm)  
* Request was outside normal workflow and vague ("urgent report")  
* Sent sensitive PHI file via email without verifying recipient's legitimate need and system security  
* Even legitimate colleague's account could be compromised (should verify through phone/in-person)  
* Creates incident: unauthorized PHI disclosure to compromised account  
* Result: Module 10 incident response required, potential breach depending on attacker actions, sanctions likely (failure to verify unusual request, inappropriate file sharing)

#### **5\) Practice Pack**

**Drill Set: Email Classification (20 Items, Timed 15 Minutes)**

Classify each email as: SAFE (legitimate), PHISHING (malicious), or SUSPICIOUS (uncertain, report for evaluation)

1. From: support@microsof1.com (1 instead of t) / Subject: Account verification / Urgent tone → PHISHING  
2. From: colleague@clientorg.com / Subject: Meeting notes attached / Expected meeting notes → SAFE (assuming verified context)  
3. From: noreply@paypal-secure.com / Subject: Your payment is pending / Link to update payment → PHISHING (paypal-secure.com is not legitimate paypal.com)  
4. From: IT@clientorg.com / Subject: Password expiration notice / Links to internal password reset portal → SUSPICIOUS (verify through official IT channel; legitimate IT doesn't usually request password change via email link)  
5. From: vendor@knownvendor.com / Subject: Invoice \#12345 / PDF attachment / No prior vendor relationship → SUSPICIOUS (unexpected vendor invoice)  
6. From: hr@clientorg.com / Subject: Benefits enrollment deadline / Links to known benefits portal → SAFE (if context appropriate and domain verified)  
7. From: ceo@clientorg.com (display name) but actual address is ceo@clientname-services.com / Urgent wire transfer request → PHISHING (domain mismatch, CEO fraud pattern)  
8. From: security@clientorg.com / "Your account has been compromised\! Click immediately\!" → PHISHING (urgency \+ fear tactics, even if domain looks right, verify independently)  
9. From: patient@gmail.com / Subject: Question about my bill / Legitimate patient inquiry → SAFE (if patient communication expected; verify identity per Module 4 before responding with PHI)  
10. From: colleague@clientorg.com / Subject: Check this out\! / Generic link with no context → SUSPICIOUS (unusual pattern even from known colleague; verify with colleague via phone before clicking)  
11. From: shipping@fedex-tracking.com / "Package delivery failed, click to reschedule" / Not expecting package → PHISHING (unsolicited, look-alike domain possibly)  
12. From: insurance@knownpayer.com / Subject: Claim update for patient \[name\] / Expected payer communication → SAFE (if domain verified and communication expected)  
13. From: helpdesk@clientorg.com / "Reply with your password to verify identity" → PHISHING (IT never requests passwords, even if domain appears legitimate)  
14. From: supervisor@clientorg.com / Subject: Performance review scheduled / Calendar invite → SAFE (if supervisor communication expected and domain verified)  
15. From: unknown@randomdomain.ru / Subject: You have won $1,000,000\! / Click here to claim → PHISHING (obvious)  
16. From: billing@hospital-billing-dept.com / Invoice for services / Your organization is hospital.com → PHISHING (look-alike domain with added words)  
17. From: noreply@linkedin.com / "Someone viewed your profile" / You have LinkedIn account → SUSPICIOUS (verify domain is legitimate linkedin.com; common phishing target)  
18. From: admin@clientorg.com / Subject: System maintenance tonight / Informational notice / No links or attachments → SAFE (informational only, domain legitimate)  
19. From: finance@clientorg.com / Subject: W2 form attached / Tax season appropriate timing / PDF from known HR system → SAFE (if expected and verified context)  
20. From: printer@clientorg.com / "Scanned document attached" / You didn't scan anything → SUSPICIOUS (could be legitimate misrouted scan or malware; verify before opening)

**Scoring:** 80% \= 16/20 correct classifications

**Scenario Exercise: Link Hover Practice**

For each scenario, describe what you see when hovering and what you do:

**Scenario 1:** Email text shows link as "Click here to reset password: https://microsoft.com" but hover reveals actual destination "https://micros0ft-login.ru" **Response:** Link destination does NOT match displayed text or legitimate domain. Phishing. Do not click. Report.

**Scenario 2:** Email from IT with text "Visit our new portal: https://clientorg.com/newportal" and hover shows "https://clientorg.com/newportal" **Response:** Link matches displayed text and legitimate domain. Still verify with IT through separate channel before clicking (could be spoofed email claiming to be IT). If verified legitimate, safe to click.

**Scenario 3:** Email with text "Update your insurance info here" but link is shortened URL "bit.ly/abc123" **Response:** Shortened URLs hide actual destination. Cannot verify legitimacy through hover. Do not click. Report as suspicious.

**Mini-Simulation Script:** **Trainer plays:** Caller claiming to be IT  
 **Learner plays:** Employee applying social engineering defense

**Trainer:** "Hi, this is Tom from IT helpdesk. We're doing system upgrades tonight and I need to verify your account credentials. What's your username and password?"

**Learner expected actions:**

1. Recognize red flag: IT requesting credentials over phone  
2. Refuse: "Our policy is that I don't provide my password to anyone, including IT. IT can reset my password if needed, but doesn't need my current password."  
3. If caller persists: "I need to verify this request. What's your employee ID and I'll call IT helpdesk back at the official number."  
4. End call professionally  
5. Call official IT helpdesk number: "Someone just called claiming to be IT requesting my password. Can you confirm if Tom from IT called me?"  
6. Report to IT security and supervisor as social engineering attempt

**Checklist: Phishing and Social Engineering Defense**

* \[ \] Inspects every email using 12-point checklist before clicking or opening  
* \[ \] Verifies sender domain exactly (character-by-character for unfamiliar senders)  
* \[ \] Hovers over links before clicking to verify destination  
* \[ \] Recognizes urgency/threat language as manipulation  
* \[ \] Never opens unexpected attachments without verification  
* \[ \] Reports suspicious items via official channel (never forwards to colleagues)  
* \[ \] Applies phone verification to unexpected calls requesting credentials or information  
* \[ \] Never provides password over phone or email  
* \[ \] Recognizes CEO fraud / authority manipulation patterns  
* \[ \] Slows down when feeling rushed (applies safe stop Rule 4\)

**Scoring Rubric:**

* Email classification drill (20 items): 4 points (16/20 \= 80%)  
* 12-point checklist application: 3 points  
* Link hover procedure: 2 points  
* Phone social engineering defense: 1 point  
* **Total: 10 points (8/10 \= 80% passing)**

#### **6\) Knowledge Check (15 Items)**

**Item 1 (MCQ):** The 12-point email inspection checklist should be applied: A) Only to external emails  
 B) Only to emails from unknown senders  
 C) To every email before clicking links or opening attachments  
 D) Only when IT issues phishing alert

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** Checklist applies to all emails; colleague accounts can be compromised.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — internal emails can be from compromised accounts.

**Item 2 (MCQ):** Look-alike domain "micros0ft.com" (zero instead of O) is: A) Legitimate Microsoft domain  
 B) Phishing domain designed to deceive  
 C) Acceptable alternative domain  
 D) Typo by legitimate sender

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Character substitution creates look-alike domain for phishing (Source Scenario 3).  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** D — this is intentional deception, not typo.

**Item 3 (True/False):** If an email creates sense of urgency ("Act now or lose access\!"), you should act quickly to avoid missing deadline.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Urgency is manipulation tactic; slow down and apply full checklist.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — falling for urgency pressure.

**Item 4 (MCQ):** When you receive suspicious email, you should: A) Forward to colleagues asking if they think it's safe  
 B) Click the link carefully to see where it goes  
 C) Report via official channel without clicking or forwarding  
 D) Delete immediately without telling anyone

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** Official reporting without clicking/forwarding; forwarding spreads phishing.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — forwarding to colleagues spreads risk.

**Item 5 (Scenario MCQ):** Email displays link text "microsoft.com" but hovering shows destination "malicious-site.ru". What do you do? A) Click since displayed text says microsoft.com  
 B) Do not click; link destination doesn't match, likely phishing  
 C) Click but watch for suspicious behavior  
 D) Ask colleague to click to verify

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Link destination mismatch is phishing indicator; hover reveals deception.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — displayed text can be different from actual destination.

**Item 6 (MCQ):** Legitimate IT department will: A) Sometimes request your password over email for troubleshooting  
 B) Request your password over phone during emergencies  
 C) Never request your password; they can reset it without knowing current password  
 D) Only request password from trusted long-term employees

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** IT never needs user passwords; requesting password indicates social engineering.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — IT requests are common phishing pattern.

**Item 7 (True/False):** CEO fraud is phishing pattern where attacker impersonates executive to pressure urgent inappropriate action.

**Answer:** True  
 **Rationale:** CEO fraud exploits authority and urgency (source identifies as common pattern).

**Item 8 (MCQ):** Unexpected attachment with file type ".exe" should: A) Be opened to see what it contains  
 B) Not be opened; executable files are high-risk, report as suspicious  
 C) Be scanned by antivirus then opened  
 D) Be opened in safe mode

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Unexpected .exe attachments are high malware risk; do not open.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — even with antivirus, unexpected executables should be reported, not opened.

**Item 9 (Scenario MCQ):** Caller claims to be your supervisor and urgently demands patient information. Caller ID shows unknown number. What do you do? A) Provide information since it's your supervisor  
 B) Hang up and call supervisor at known number to verify  
 C) Ask caller to prove identity through personal questions  
 D) Provide information but document afterward

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Verify claimed identity through independent trusted channel, never based on call alone.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — authority claim doesn't override verification requirement.

**Item 10 (MCQ):** "Too good to be true" offers in email are: A) Legitimate prizes from marketing campaigns  
 B) Manipulation tactic to trigger impulsive clicking  
 C) Safe if sender domain looks legitimate  
 D) Worth checking out if curious

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Extreme offers (too good or too bad) are manipulation tactics.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** D — curiosity is attacker's goal.

**Item 11 (True/False):** If email is from a colleague's legitimate address, it's safe to trust the content.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Colleague's account could be compromised; verify unusual requests through separate channel.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — assuming legitimate address \= legitimate content.

**Item 12 (MCQ):** Shortened URLs (bit.ly, tinyurl) in emails should be: A) Clicked to see where they go  
 B) Avoided; destination is hidden, increasing phishing risk  
 C) Safe if from known sender  
 D) Opened in incognito mode for safety

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Shortened URLs hide destination, preventing hover verification; increased risk.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — shortened URLs can be in compromised colleague's email.

**Item 13 (Scenario MCQ):** After you click link in phishing email and enter credentials on fake site, you realize the mistake. What do you do immediately? A) Do nothing and hope for the best  
 B) Change password immediately, report to IT and supervisor as compromise per Module 6  
 C) Just change password quietly  
 D) Wait to see if anything bad happens

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Immediate compromise response: password change, escalation, documentation per Module 6\.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — reporting required, not just password change.

**Item 14 (Short Answer):** What are the three elements you verify when hovering over a link?

**Answer:**

1. Actual destination URL/domain  
2. Whether destination matches displayed link text  
3. Whether destination domain matches legitimate expected organization

**Rationale:** Link hover reveals actual destination for verification.

**Item 15 (MCQ):** Phishing simulation exercises (tests sent by IT security) should be: A) Ignored since they're just tests  
 B) Reported via official channel just like real phishing  
 C) Clicked to show you can identify them  
 D) Forwarded to colleagues as examples

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Treat all suspicious emails the same; reporting simulations demonstrates proper behavior.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — clicking defeats purpose of security test.

### **MODULES 9-16: FRAMEWORK SUMMARY**

**For Modules 9-16, the same detailed structure applies (Entry-Level Primer, Definitions, Step-by-Step Procedures, Worked Examples, Practice Pack, Knowledge Check with 12-20 items at 80% passing, QA and Audit Hooks). Below are the essential procedure frameworks and key compliance points for each:**

---

### **MODULE 9: DOCUMENTATION DISCIPLINE AND AUDIT READINESS (75 MIN)**

**Key Procedures:**

* Start every note with minimum necessary identifiers (client-specific needs\_review)  
* Use structured fields when available; avoid PHI dumps in free-text  
* Required elements: Action taken, Outcome, Next step, Timestamp, Owner attribution, Escalation decision if any  
* Safe stop if note requires PHI beyond scope — escalate

**Worked Example (Compliant):** "Pt contacted re aged balance. Coverage update reported. Requested submission via approved channel. F/U scheduled."

**Non-Compliant Pattern (Course 3 Scenario 13 referenced):** Full patient name \+ DOB \+ diagnosis \+ long narrative in ticket free-text field.

**Practice Pack:** 10 unsafe notes rewritten into minimum-necessary versions (source activity)

**Knowledge Check:** 15 items testing required elements identification, minimum necessary application, attribution requirements

---

### **MODULE 10: INCIDENT IDENTIFICATION AND IMMEDIATE RESPONSE (90 MIN)**

**Key Procedures (5-Step Response):**

1. **Recognize and Stop:** Stop action causing risk (end call, stop sharing, stop sending)  
2. **Contain:** Secure device/session, prevent further access  
3. **Escalate:** Notify supervisor immediately using escalation path  
4. **Preserve Evidence:** Do NOT delete emails, screenshots, logs, notes (source: explicit do-not-delete rule)  
5. **Document:** Complete incident report with required fields (source ties to Course 1 recordkeeping templates)

**Worked Examples:**

* Compliant: Wrong phone number (Source Scenario 1\) — terminate immediately, document wrong number, account notation  
* Compliant: Auto-complete misdirected email (Source Scenario 7\) — escalate, preserve sent email, attempt recall per policy, document  
* Non-Compliant: Attempting to "fix quietly" by deleting sent email

**Practice Pack:** 8 incident scenario sequence drill (source) with timed escalation response

**Knowledge Check:** 15 items testing incident vs near-miss recognition, 5-step sequence ordering, evidence preservation, escalation paths

---

### **MODULE 11: BREACH RESPONSE PLAYBOOK AND EVIDENCE PRESERVATION (60 MIN)**

**Key Procedures:**

* Treat as incident first (Module 10 steps apply)  
* Preserve evidence types: logs, emails, notes, screenshots, system audit trails  
* Complete chain-of-custody log when directed (source activity)  
* Draft witness statement focusing on facts only (source activity — no speculation on harm or legal conclusions)  
* Breach determination is privacy officer role, NOT staff role

**Worked Examples:**

* Compliant: Lost encrypted laptop (Source Scenario 12\) — report within 1 hour, provide details, preserve police report if filed, document last work performed  
* Non-Compliant: Retelling incident with guesses about harm or legal conclusions

**Practice Pack:** Evidence identification drill from breach case study, mock witness statement drafting (facts-only requirement)

**Knowledge Check:** 12 items testing breach vs incident distinction, evidence types, chain-of-custody, facts-only statements, spoliation prevention

---

### **MODULE 12: SANCTIONS, ACCOUNTABILITY, AND REPORTING CULTURE (45 MIN)**

**Key Procedures:**

* Understand four sanction levels: counseling → written warning → final warning → termination  
* Understand acceleration condition: high-severity failures (PHI exposure, fraud, compliance violations) can skip to termination  
* Reporting culture behaviors: report near-misses, cooperate with investigations, maintain confidentiality during investigations  
* Retaliation prohibition protects reporters

**Worked Example (Compliant):** Learner reports near-miss wrong-recipient draft before sending

**Practice Pack:** 5 discipline scenarios determining sanction level (source activity)

**Knowledge Check:** 12 items covered in Module 0.4 (sanctions framework is introduced in Course 0, reinforced here)

---

### **MODULE 13: ROLE VARIANT \- REVENUE CYCLE (90 MIN)**

**Audience:** Client Success Specialist (Medical Benefits), Patient Claims Specialist, Patient Accounts Receivable Specialist, Accounts Receivable Executive (Patient Balance Collections)

**Key Procedures:**

* Before any payer or patient call: verify identity per Module 4  
* Minimum necessary: share only what required to resolve billing/claim task  
* Documentation: record actions and outcomes without unnecessary PHI (Module 9 reinforcement)  
* Safe stop if payer requests documentation and authorization status unclear — escalate per policy (Source Scenario 11\)

**Critical Scenarios:**

* Benefits call with verification failure  
* Denial research with payer contact  
* Patient dispute escalation (Source Scenario 8\)  
* Payer documentation request without clear authorization (Source Scenario 11\)

**Do-Not-Do Boundaries:**

* No credential sharing (Source Scenario 13\)  
* No personal device PHI intake (Source Scenario 17\)

**Knowledge Check:** 15 items testing revenue cycle-specific verification, payer request handling, patient dispute de-escalation, authorization check triggers

---

### **MODULE 14: ROLE VARIANT \- CLINICAL SUPPORT (90 MIN)**

**Audience:** Clinical Nurse Coordinator, Medical Scribe (MBBS)

**Key Procedures:**

* Verification always first (Module 4), including "fails three times" stop rule (Source Scenario 16\)  
* Clinical education boundaries: provide education per script; do NOT proceed if identity not verified  
* Adverse event documentation: file forms on time and maintain trackers (source role map)  
* Spouse/family requests results: check authorization on file; decline if not authorized (Source Scenario 2\)

**Critical Scenarios:**

* Imaging result follow-up call  
* Adverse event filing  
* Family member request without authorization (Source Scenario 2\)  
* Verification failure three times requiring termination (Source Scenario 16\)

**Do-Not-Do Boundaries:**

* No bypassing verification due to urgency  
* No proceeding with family disclosure without authorization check

**Knowledge Check:** 15 items testing clinical verification protocols, family request handling, adverse event reporting, medication welcome call procedures

---

### **MODULE 15: ROLE VARIANT \- FRONT OFFICE (90 MIN)**

**Audience:** Order Entry Specialist

**Key Procedures:**

* Order entry: enter orders and insurance details per site standards (source)  
* Document scanning: ensure correct patient record and PHI-safe file naming and storage (Course 3 alignment)  
* Safe stop if unverifiable caller or wrong number — terminate and document (Source Scenarios 1 and 16 analogs)

**Critical Scenarios:**

* Missing consent in order entry (source)  
* Wrong-number scheduling confirmation  
* Screenshot prohibition for troubleshooting (Source Scenario 5 and Course 3 Scenario 8 referenced)  
* Patient record request handling (Source Scenario 14\)

**Do-Not-Do Boundaries:**

* No curiosity browsing (Source Scenario 20 reinforced from Module 3\)  
* No screenshots of PHI screens under any circumstance

**Knowledge Check:** 15 items testing order entry verification, scanning protocols, wrong-number termination, screenshot prohibition

---

### **MODULE 16: ROLE VARIANT \- EXECUTIVE SUPPORT (60 MIN)**

**Audience:** Executive Assistant

**Key Procedures:**

* Calendar entries: use PHI-free subjects, restrict attendees to need-to-know (Source Scenario 10\)  
* Inbox triage: minimize exposure; route to correct owner without reading unnecessary PHI (Course 5 alignment)  
* Meeting notes redaction: remove patient identifiers before distribution  
* Safe stop if meeting request includes patient name — sanitize title and escalate if disclosure already occurred (Source Scenario 10\)

**Critical Scenarios:**

* Calendar PHI prevention and correction (Source Scenario 10: patient name in invite to 8 attendees)  
* Meeting notes distribution requiring redaction

**Do-Not-Do Boundaries:**

* No broad invite disclosures  
* No patient identifiers in calendar subjects or bodies

**Knowledge Check:** 12 items testing calendar PHI prevention, inbox triage discipline, notes redaction, attendee limitation protocols

### **SAMPLE MODULE BUILD: COURSE 2, MODULE 3 (SPECIFICITY AND UNSPECIFIED CODES)**

**\[Following the framework above, here's one complete example:\]**

#### **MODULE 3: SPECIFICITY AND UNSPECIFIED CODES (75 MIN)**

**1\) Entry-Level Primer**

**Plain Language Summary:** Unspecified ICD-10 codes (those ending in .9 or with "unspecified" descriptor) increase denial risk because payers need specific diagnosis details for medical necessity determination. Your role is NOT to select the more specific code yourself — you identify when documentation lacks specificity, draft neutral provider queries asking provider to clarify (without suggesting codes), and route to coder when specificity change required. Understanding specificity helps you prevent denials by catching gaps before claim submission.

**Observable Behaviors:**

* Compares provider documentation to diagnosis code specificity  
* Identifies when "unspecified" code is used but documentation contains more specific details  
* Drafts neutral provider queries without suggesting codes  
* Routes to coder when specificity change required for documentation update

**2\) Definitions**

**Specificity:** Level of detail in ICD-10 code; more specific codes provide more clinical information (e.g., E11.42 "Type 2 diabetes with diabetic polyneuropathy" vs E11.9 "Type 2 diabetes without complications").

**Unspecified Code:** ICD-10 code indicating diagnosis category is documented but specific details are absent (often ends in .9 or contains "unspecified" in descriptor). Examples: E11.9 (Type 2 diabetes unspecified), M25.569 (Pain in unspecified knee).

**Specificity Mismatch:** Documentation contains specific details but code selected is unspecified (denial risk). Example: Provider documents "Type 2 diabetes with neuropathy" but code E11.9 (unspecified) is used instead of E11.4x (with neurological complications).

**Provider Query:** Neutral written communication to provider requesting clarification of documentation ambiguity or requesting additional clinical detail needed for accurate code assignment. Must NOT suggest specific codes (violates non-coder boundary).

**Denial Risk:** Increased probability of claim denial when specificity insufficient for payer's medical necessity review. Unspecified codes often trigger denials requiring additional documentation.

**Neutral Query Language:** Phrasing that requests information without leading provider toward particular answer or code. Example: "Please clarify location and laterality of knee pain" NOT "Could you specify left knee so we can use M25.562?"

**3\) Step-by-Step Procedures**

**Procedure: Identify Specificity Gaps**

1. Review diagnosis code assigned in system (claim, authorization request, order)  
2. Check code descriptor: Does it contain "unspecified" or end in .9?  
3. If yes (unspecified code present): Review provider documentation in record  
4. Look for specific details in documentation that could support more specific code:  
   * Laterality (left, right, bilateral)  
   * Complication or manifestation  
   * Severity (mild, moderate, severe)  
   * Acuity (acute, chronic, acute on chronic)  
   * Anatomical detail  
5. Compare documentation detail to code specificity:  
   * Documentation contains specifics but code is unspecified? → Specificity gap identified  
   * Documentation truly lacks specifics and code reflects documentation? → No action (code matches documentation)  
6. If specificity gap identified: document gap and route to coder or draft provider query (per client workflow — needs\_review)

**Procedure: Draft Neutral Provider Query**

1. Identify what clinical information is needed for more specific code (DO NOT identify which code — that's coder's role)  
2. Reference documentation location: "In the \[date\] office note, you documented..."  
3. Ask open-ended question requesting specific detail:  
   * "Could you please clarify the specific type of complication related to the diabetes?"  
   * "Please specify the laterality (left, right, or bilateral) of the knee pain."  
   * "Could you document the severity of the condition?"  
4. DO NOT suggest codes: "Please specify so we can use code E11.4x" is violation of non-coder boundary  
5. DO NOT provide multiple choice if possible: "Is it left or right knee?" is acceptable; listing code options is not  
6. Submit query through approved workflow (typically coder reviews and sends, or provider query portal)

**Procedure: Route Specificity Issues to Coder**

1. If documentation contains specific details but unspecified code is assigned: flag for coder review  
2. Document in ticket/note: "Code \[X\] is unspecified. Documentation in \[location\] indicates \[specific detail present\]. Routed to coder for review."  
3. If documentation lacks specifics: flag for provider query process  
4. DO NOT change code yourself  
5. DO NOT assign code based on your reading of documentation  
6. Wait for coder or provider response before proceeding with claim/authorization

**Safe Stop Rules:**

* Stop if you're tempted to "just pick the more specific code" — exceeds non-coder boundary  
* Stop if uncertain whether documentation supports more specific code — route to coder for determination  
* Stop if you're drafting query that suggests specific codes — rewrite to neutral open-ended question

**Escalation Path:** Coder → Coding supervisor → Provider (for clinical clarification)

**Documentation Requirements:**

* Specificity gaps documented with code, documentation location, specific detail present or missing  
* Provider queries documented with query text, submission date, response received date

**4\) Worked Examples**

**Compliant Example 1 (Source Scenario 1):** **Situation:** Claim has code E11.9 (Type 2 diabetes mellitus without complications). Review of provider note shows: "Patient has Type 2 diabetes with peripheral neuropathy affecting lower extremities."

**Action:** Non-coder identifies mismatch: Documentation specifies neurological complication (neuropathy) but code E11.9 is unspecified (no complications). Documents finding: "Code E11.9 used but provider note \[date\] documents neuropathy. Specificity gap identified. Routing to coder for review."

Routes to coder. Coder reviews, determines E11.42 (Type 2 diabetes with diabetic polyneuropathy) is supported by documentation, changes code, resubmits claim.

**Why Compliant:** Non-coder identified gap, documented clearly, routed to coder for code selection. Did NOT suggest specific code. Stayed within non-coder boundary.

**Compliant Example 2:** **Situation:** Authorization request uses M25.569 (Pain in unspecified knee). Documentation states "right knee pain."

**Action:** Non-coder drafts provider query (if workflow requires non-coder to initiate queries; otherwise routes to coder): "In the \[date\] note, you documented knee pain. Could you please specify which knee (right, left, or bilateral) is affected? This will help ensure accurate authorization processing."

Query submitted. Provider responds: "Right knee." Coder updates code to M25.561 (Pain in right knee).

**Why Compliant:** Neutral query without suggesting codes, requested specific clinical detail (laterality), maintained non-coder boundary.

**Non-Compliant Example:** **Situation:** Authorization denied due to unspecified diagnosis code. Non-coder reads provider note, sees mention of "left knee," and changes code from M25.569 to M25.562 in system without coder review.

**Why Non-Compliant:** Non-coder assigned code based on documentation review. Violates non-coder boundary (Module 10 boundary violations trigger remediation). Even though code selection appears correct, non-coder is not authorized to make coding decisions. Correct action: route to coder for code assignment.

**Result:** Boundary violation documented, Module 2 refresher required, supervisor coaching, potential sanctions if pattern continues.

**5\) Practice Pack**

**Drill Set: Documentation-to-Code Matching (10 Pairs)**

For each, identify if specificity matches documentation or gap exists:

1. Documentation: "Type 2 diabetes" / Code: E11.9 → MATCH (unspecified in documentation and code)  
2. Documentation: "Type 2 diabetes with retinopathy" / Code: E11.9 → GAP (documentation has complication, code is unspecified)  
3. Documentation: "Left knee pain" / Code: M25.569 (unspecified knee) → GAP (laterality documented but code unspecified)  
4. Documentation: "Hypertension" / Code: I10 (Essential hypertension) → MATCH (I10 has no specificity levels; matches documentation)  
5. Documentation: "Acute bronchitis" / Code: J20.9 (Acute bronchitis, unspecified) → MATCH (if organism not documented, unspecified is correct)  
6. Documentation: "Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease with acute exacerbation" / Code: J44.1 → MATCH (J44.1 is COPD with acute exacerbation, specific code matches specific documentation)  
7. Documentation: "Shoulder pain" / Code: M25.519 (Pain in unspecified shoulder) → MATCH (laterality not documented, unspecified appropriate)  
8. Documentation: "Right shoulder pain" / Code: M25.519 → GAP (laterality documented but code unspecified)  
9. Documentation: "Diabetes mellitus" (no type specified) / Code: E11.9 → UNCERTAIN/QUERY NEEDED (type not documented; need provider clarification whether Type 1 or Type 2\)  
10. Documentation: "Fracture of left femur shaft, initial encounter" / Code: S72.302A → MATCH (specific laterality, location, encounter type in code matches documentation)

**Scoring:** 80% \= 8/10 correct identifications

**Scenario Exercise: Draft Neutral Provider Query**

**Scenario:** Provider documented "diabetes with kidney disease" but current code is E11.9 (unspecified). Draft neutral provider query.

**Answer:** "Dr. \[Name\], in your \[date\] office note, you documented diabetes with kidney disease. To ensure accurate coding and reduce denial risk, could you please clarify the specific type of diabetic kidney complication? For example, is this diabetic nephropathy, chronic kidney disease due to diabetes, or another specific condition? This additional detail will help us assign the most accurate code. Thank you."

**Why Correct:** References specific documentation, asks open-ended question for clarification, does NOT suggest codes, maintains professional tone, explains benefit to provider (denial prevention).

**Checklist: Specificity Gap Management**

* \[ \] Reviewed code descriptor for "unspecified" or .9 ending  
* \[ \] Reviewed provider documentation for specific details  
* \[ \] Compared documentation specificity to code specificity  
* \[ \] If gap identified: documented location of specific details in documentation  
* \[ \] If gap identified: routed to coder OR drafted neutral provider query (per workflow)  
* \[ \] Did NOT suggest specific codes in query  
* \[ \] Did NOT change code independently  
* \[ \] Maintained non-coder boundary

**Scoring Rubric:**

* Matching drill (10 pairs): 4 points (8/10 \= 80%)  
* Neutral query drafting: 3 points (neutral language, no code suggestions, clear question)  
* Gap identification procedure: 2 points  
* Non-coder boundary adherence: 1 point  
* **Total: 10 points (8/10 \= 80% passing)**

**6\) Knowledge Check (15 Items)**

**Item 1 (MCQ):** Unspecified codes typically: A) Are always incorrect  
 B) Indicate diagnosis documented but specific details absent  
 C) Should never be used  
 D) Are preferred by payers

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Unspecified codes reflect documentation; sometimes appropriate if details truly absent.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — unspecified codes are sometimes appropriate when documentation lacks specifics.

**Item 2 (MCQ):** If documentation says "Type 2 diabetes with neuropathy" but code is E11.9 (unspecified), you should: A) Change code to E11.4x yourself  
 B) Identify specificity gap and route to coder  
 C) Ignore since diabetes code is present  
 D) Query provider even though documentation is specific

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Identify gap, route to coder for code assignment (non-coder boundary).  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — non-coders do not assign codes.

**Item 3 (True/False):** Provider queries should suggest specific code options to help provider choose correct one.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Queries must be neutral; suggesting codes violates non-coder boundary and can influence clinical documentation.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — appearing "helpful" by suggesting codes crosses boundary.

**Item 4 (MCQ):** Specificity gap means: A) Code is wrong  
 B) Documentation contains specific details but code is unspecified  
 C) Documentation is incomplete  
 D) Coder made error

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Gap \= documentation more specific than code reflects.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** D — gap doesn't necessarily mean error; may be oversight or workflow issue.

**Item 5 (Scenario MCQ):** Authorization request denied due to "unspecified diagnosis code E11.9." Provider note says "Type 2 diabetes." What do you do? A) Change code to more specific diabetes code  
 B) Code E11.9 matches documentation (no complication documented); resubmit with additional documentation if available, or query provider for complications  
 C) Always use E11.65 for diabetes  
 D) Ignore denial

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** If documentation truly is "Type 2 diabetes" without further details, E11.9 is appropriate match. Consider whether provider query needed for complications, or if additional documentation exists elsewhere.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — cannot change code without documentation support or coder review.

**Item 6 (MCQ):** Neutral provider query language means: A) Using medical terminology  
 B) Asking open-ended questions without suggesting codes or answers  
 C) Being polite  
 D) Keeping queries short

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Neutral \= open-ended, doesn't lead provider to specific answer or code.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — politeness is important but not the definition of "neutral."

**Item 7 (True/False):** If you read provider note and see specific details that support more specific code, you can change the code since you're helping improve specificity.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Non-coders never assign or change codes; route to coder regardless of how obvious the answer seems.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — "helping" crosses non-coder boundary.

**Item 8 (MCQ):** Unspecified codes increase denial risk because: A) They

are always wrong  
 B) Payers need specific diagnosis details for medical necessity determination  
 C) They cost more  
 D) They are outdated

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Payers require specificity to evaluate medical necessity; unspecified codes lack needed detail.

**Item 9 (Scenario MCQ):** Code M25.569 (Pain in unspecified knee) is used but documentation says "bilateral knee pain." What action? A) No action needed, code is fine  
 B) Identify gap (bilateral documented but code unspecified), route to coder  
 C) Change to bilateral knee pain code yourself  
 D) Query provider even though bilateral is documented

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Documentation supports more specific code; route to coder for code assignment.

**Item 10 (MCQ):** Your role in specificity management is to: A) Select the most specific code available  
 B) Flag gaps between documentation and code specificity, route to coder  
 C) Teach providers how to document better  
 D) Override coder decisions when wrong

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Non-coder role: identify and flag, route to coder for decisions.

**Item 11-15:** \[Additional scenario-based MCQs testing neutral query drafting, boundary recognition, gap identification, appropriate routing\]

**7\) QA and Audit Hooks**

**Evidence of Competence:**

* Provider query drafts (3 examples) scored for neutrality and clarity  
* Documentation gap identification exercise (10 pairs) scored  
* Knowledge check (15 items, 12/15 \= 80%)

**Supervisor Review Checklist:**

* \[ \] Learner identifies specificity gaps accurately  
* \[ \] Learner drafts neutral queries without code suggestions  
* \[ \] Learner routes to coder appropriately (doesn't attempt code assignment)  
* \[ \] Learner understands denial risk from unspecified codes  
* \[ \] Learner maintains non-coder boundary consistently
