## **COURSE 2: ICD-10 CODING OVERVIEW FOR OPERATIONS TEAMS**

### **MODULE 7: MEDICAL NECESSITY AND DIAGNOSIS SELECTION (90 MIN)**

#### **1\) Entry-Level Primer**

**Plain Language Summary:** Medical necessity (diagnosis justifies service clinically) is distinct from coverage (payer policy allows payment). Your role is recognizing when diagnosis-service mismatches create denial risk, NOT determining whether service is medically necessary (that's provider/coder role). LCD (Local Coverage Determination) and NCD (National Coverage Determination) are payer policies specifying which diagnoses support coverage for specific services — these require payer-policy confirmation (needs\_review per client). Understanding this distinction prevents promising patients "insurance will cover it" when you cannot make that determination.

**Observable Behaviors:**

* Compares diagnosis context to requested service context  
* Identifies potential denial risk when diagnosis doesn't obviously support service  
* Routes to authorization/coding team when denial risk suspected  
* Never promises coverage or approval to patients  
* Distinguishes between "clinically appropriate" (medical necessity) and "payer will pay" (coverage)

#### **2\) Definitions**

**Medical Necessity:** Clinical standard requiring service to be appropriate for diagnosis, consistent with standards of care, not experimental, and expected to improve patient condition. Determined by provider based on clinical judgment.

**Coverage:** Payer policy determination of whether service will be paid based on contract terms, LCD/NCD requirements, and diagnosis code submitted. Determined by payer, not provider or operational staff.

**Medical Necessity vs Coverage Distinction (Critical):** Service can be medically necessary (clinically appropriate) but not covered (payer won't pay). Example: Provider determines imaging is medically necessary for patient's condition, but payer's LCD doesn't include that diagnosis as covered indication for that imaging type. Service is medically necessary but may not be covered.

**LCD (Local Coverage Determination):** Medicare Administrative Contractor (MAC) policy specifying coverage criteria for specific services in specific geographic regions. Includes covered diagnosis codes, frequency limits, documentation requirements. (needs\_review for client-specific LCD access and application)

**NCD (National Coverage Determination):** Medicare national policy covering all regions. Takes precedence over LCDs. Specifies coverage criteria for specific services nationwide. (needs\_review for client-specific NCD access and application)

**Prior Authorization:** Payer's advance approval required before service delivery to confirm coverage. Often triggered by diagnosis-service combination requiring medical necessity review.

**Denial Risk Indicators:** Diagnosis-service combinations likely to result in claim denial (unspecified diagnosis for high-tech service, diagnosis doesn't match body system of procedure, frequency exceeds typical coverage limits, experimental service codes).

**Coverage Policy Resources (needs\_review):** Client-specific tools for accessing LCD/NCD and payer policies. May include payer portals, coverage databases, or internal policy libraries. Exact resources and access procedures need\_review per client.

#### **3\) Step-by-Step Procedures**

**Procedure: Identify Potential Denial Risk in Diagnosis-Service Pairing**

1. **Review service requested:**

   * What procedure/service is ordered? (CPT code if available, or service description)  
   * What is clinical purpose stated by provider?  
2. **Review diagnosis code(s) associated with service:**

   * What diagnosis code(s) are listed as reason for service?  
   * Does diagnosis relate to body system of service? (Example: knee pain diagnosis for knee MRI \= related; diabetes diagnosis for knee MRI \= questionable relationship)  
3. **Apply common-sense pairing assessment:**

   * Does diagnosis logically support need for this service?  
   * Example matches: Chest pain → cardiac testing; Back pain → spine imaging; Diabetes → A1C lab  
   * Example mismatches: Hypertension → knee MRI; Ankle sprain → brain CT  
4. **Check for high-risk indicators:**

   * Unspecified diagnosis (ends in .9) for high-cost or high-tech service  
   * Diagnosis doesn't match anatomical area of service  
   * Frequency of service seems excessive for diagnosis  
   * Service description includes "experimental" or "investigational"  
5. **If potential denial risk identified:**

   * Document concern: "Service \[X\] requested with diagnosis \[Y\]. Potential coverage risk due to \[mismatch/unspecified code/frequency\]. Routing to authorization team for policy review."  
   * Route to authorization coordinator or coding team per workflow (needs\_review)  
   * DO NOT tell patient service is "not covered" — you are flagging for expert review, not making coverage determination  
6. **If confident pairing is appropriate:**

   * Proceed with normal workflow  
   * Still avoid promising coverage ("This should be covered" is not appropriate; "I've submitted for authorization review" is appropriate)

**Procedure: Access and Interpret Coverage Policies (needs\_review per client tools)**

**Note:** Exact procedures depend on client's policy access tools and workflow. General framework:

1. **Identify payer and service:**

   * What insurance does patient have?  
   * What specific service needs coverage verification?  
2. **Access coverage policy tool:**

   * Client may provide: payer portals, LCD/NCD databases, internal policy library, authorization software with embedded coverage rules  
   * Navigate to relevant service category (imaging, labs, DME, procedures)  
3. **Locate coverage criteria:**

   * Search by CPT code or service description  
   * Identify covered diagnosis codes listed in policy  
   * Note frequency/quantity limits if specified  
   * Note documentation requirements if specified  
4. **Compare patient's diagnosis to covered diagnosis list:**

   * Is patient's diagnosis code on covered list? → Likely covered (still requires verification)  
   * Is patient's diagnosis code absent from list? → Potential denial risk, escalate for policy interpretation  
   * Is policy ambiguous or unclear? → Escalate, do not guess  
5. **Document findings:**

   * "Per \[payer\] LCD, diagnosis \[X\] is listed as covered indication for service \[Y\]. Proceeding with authorization request."  
   * "Per \[payer\] LCD, diagnosis \[X\] is not listed as covered indication for service \[Y\]. Escalating to authorization team for policy review and possible provider query."  
6. **Safe stops:**

   * If cannot access policy: escalate to supervisor for access assistance  
   * If policy is unclear or contradictory: escalate to authorization specialist, do not interpret independently  
   * If policy seems to conflict with medical necessity: escalate (provider/authorization team resolves, not operational staff)

**Procedure: Communicate Coverage Uncertainty to Patients (Safe Language)**

**Never say:**

* "Your insurance will cover this" (you cannot guarantee)  
* "This is not covered" (you cannot definitively determine)  
* "The doctor ordered it so it's covered" (medical necessity ≠ automatic coverage)  
* "It's medically necessary so insurance has to pay" (conflates medical necessity with coverage)

**Do say:**

* "I've submitted this for authorization review. The payer will determine coverage based on your policy."  
* "I've identified a potential coverage question and escalated to our authorization team to review the policy."  
* "Coverage depends on your specific insurance policy. I recommend calling your insurance to verify coverage for this service."  
* "Authorization is pending. We'll notify you once we receive the payer's decision."

**If patient asks directly "Will my insurance cover this?":** "Coverage depends on your specific policy and the payer's determination. I've submitted for authorization \[or: this is being reviewed for authorization\]. You can also call your insurance using the number on your card to verify coverage for \[service\]. We'll let you know what the payer decides."

**Safe Stop Rules:**

* Stop if diagnosis-service pairing seems questionable — escalate for policy review before proceeding  
* Stop if patient asks for coverage guarantee — use safe language, never promise coverage  
* Stop if policy interpretation is unclear — escalate to authorization specialist  
* Stop if tempted to say "medically necessary means covered" — these are separate determinations

**Escalation Path:** Authorization coordinator → Coding supervisor (for diagnosis questions) → Payer representative (for policy clarification)

**Documentation Requirements:**

* Denial risk flags documented with specific concern (diagnosis mismatch, unspecified code, frequency issue)  
* Policy review requests documented with service, diagnosis, payer, policy question  
* Patient communications documented using safe language (no coverage promises)

#### **4\) Worked Examples**

**Compliant Example 1 \- Denial Risk Prediction:** Order: MRI of lumbar spine  
 Diagnosis: M54.5 (Low back pain)  
 Payer: Medicare

Employee review:

* Service (spine MRI) matches diagnosis body system (back pain) ✓  
* Diagnosis is commonly covered indication for lumbar MRI per general knowledge  
* Diagnosis is specific (not unspecified .9 code) ✓  
* No obvious red flags

Employee action: Proceeds with authorization request submission. Documents: "Authorization request submitted for lumbar spine MRI with diagnosis M54.5. Diagnosis appropriate for service." Does NOT tell patient "This will be covered" but says "I've submitted for authorization. You'll be notified of the decision."

**Why Compliant:** Appropriate pairing assessment, proceeded appropriately, used safe language with patient, avoided coverage guarantee.

**Compliant Example 2 \- Denial Risk Escalation:** Order: MRI brain  
 Diagnosis: E11.9 (Type 2 diabetes mellitus without complications)  
 Payer: Commercial insurance

Employee review:

* Service (brain MRI) does NOT match diagnosis body system (diabetes) ✗  
* Diagnosis doesn't obviously support need for brain imaging  
* Potential diagnosis mismatch or documentation gap

Employee action: Documents concern: "Brain MRI requested with diagnosis E11.9 (diabetes). Diagnosis doesn't appear to support neurological imaging. Possible documentation gap or diagnosis mismatch. Routing to authorization team for policy review. May require provider query to clarify clinical indication." Routes to authorization coordinator.

Authorization coordinator reviews provider order notes, discovers patient has diabetic neuropathy affecting vision (documented elsewhere but not captured in diagnosis code). Coordinates with coder to add appropriate neurological complication code. Authorization proceeds with corrected diagnosis.

**Why Compliant:** Identified mismatch, escalated appropriately, did not independently determine "not covered," allowed authorization specialists to investigate and resolve.

**Non-Compliant Example (Source Scenario 19 Context):** Patient calls: "Will my insurance cover the CT scan Dr. Smith ordered?"

Employee: "Yes, the doctor ordered it so it's medically necessary. Your insurance has to cover it."

Patient receives CT scan. Claim denies because diagnosis code doesn't meet payer's coverage criteria per LCD. Patient receives bill for $2,500. Patient complains: "Your staff told me it would be covered\!"

**Why Non-Compliant:**

* Promised coverage without verification (cannot guarantee)  
* Conflated medical necessity (provider's determination) with coverage (payer's determination)  
* Source Scenario 19 addresses coverage limitation vs coding error distinction  
* Created patient expectation that wasn't met  
* Organizational liability for misinformation

**Correct response should have been:** "Coverage depends on your insurance policy. I've submitted for authorization. You can also call your insurance to verify coverage. We'll notify you once we receive the payer's decision."

**Result:** Patient complaint, supervisor coaching on safe language, documentation of communication failure, possible financial adjustment to patient account depending on organizational policy, refresher training on medical necessity vs coverage distinction.

#### **5\) Practice Pack**

**Drill Set: Denial Risk Prediction (12 Scenarios)**

For each diagnosis-service pair, classify as: LOW RISK (appropriate pairing), MODERATE RISK (uncertain, needs review), HIGH RISK (likely denial)

1. Diagnosis: M25.561 (Pain in right knee) / Service: MRI right knee → LOW RISK (diagnosis matches service)  
2. Diagnosis: E11.9 (Diabetes unspecified) / Service: Cardiac catheterization → MODERATE/HIGH RISK (diagnosis doesn't match service; possible documentation gap)  
3. Diagnosis: I10 (Essential hypertension) / Service: Blood pressure monitoring → LOW RISK (diagnosis matches service)  
4. Diagnosis: Z23 (Encounter for immunization) / Service: Flu vaccine → LOW RISK (appropriate)  
5. Diagnosis: R07.9 (Chest pain, unspecified) / Service: Cardiac stress test → MODERATE RISK (unspecified diagnosis for significant service; may need more specific diagnosis)  
6. Diagnosis: J44.1 (COPD with acute exacerbation) / Service: Chest X-ray → LOW RISK (diagnosis supports imaging)  
7. Diagnosis: G43.909 (Migraine, unspecified) / Service: Brain MRI → MODERATE RISK (unspecified diagnosis; MRI often requires specific indication)  
8. Diagnosis: Z00.00 (Encounter for general adult medical examination without abnormal findings) / Service: Colonoscopy → MODERATE RISK (screening vs diagnostic distinction matters for coverage)  
9. Diagnosis: M79.3 (Panniculitis, unspecified) / Service: Knee replacement → HIGH RISK (diagnosis doesn't support major surgery indication)  
10. Diagnosis: E11.65 (Type 2 diabetes with hyperglycemia) / Service: Glucose monitoring supplies → LOW RISK (diagnosis supports service)  
11. Diagnosis: S82.001A (Unspecified fracture of right patella, initial encounter) / Service: Knee X-ray → LOW RISK (diagnosis supports imaging)  
12. Diagnosis: F41.9 (Anxiety disorder, unspecified) / Service: Cardiac catheterization → HIGH RISK (psychiatric diagnosis doesn't support cardiac procedure; likely documentation error or wrong diagnosis submitted)

**Scoring:** 80% \= 10/12 correct risk classifications

**Scenario Exercise: Patient Communication with Safe Language**

**Scenario:** Patient calls: "I had an MRI last week. My doctor says I need surgery. Will my insurance cover the surgery?"

**Your response (write what you would say):**

**Sample Compliant Response:** "Surgery authorization depends on your specific insurance policy and their review of the medical necessity. Dr. \[Name\]'s office will submit an authorization request with the diagnosis and procedure details. The insurance company will review and make a coverage determination. I recommend calling your insurance using the number on your card to ask about coverage for the specific surgery code, and you can ask about your deductible and co-insurance as well. We'll notify you once we receive the authorization decision from your insurance."

**Why Compliant:**

* No coverage guarantee given  
* Explains authorization process  
* Directs patient to their insurance for coverage questions  
* Distinguishes between submission and approval  
* Acknowledges patient will be notified of decision

**Non-Compliant Response:** "Yes, if the doctor says you need it, insurance will cover it."

**Why Non-Compliant:**

* False coverage guarantee  
* Conflates medical necessity with coverage  
* Creates expectation that may not be met

**Mini-Simulation Script:** **Trainer plays:** Patient asking coverage questions  
 **Learner plays:** Employee using safe language

**Round 1:** **Trainer:** "My doctor ordered blood work. Is that covered?"

**Learner expected response:** "Coverage depends on your insurance policy. I've submitted the order. You can call your insurance to verify coverage for lab work with the diagnosis your doctor documented. We'll bill your insurance and notify you of any patient responsibility."

**Round 2:** **Trainer:** "But the doctor said I need it. That means it's covered, right?"

**Learner expected response:** "The doctor has determined the lab work is medically necessary for your care. Coverage is a separate determination made by your insurance company based on your policy. They'll review the order and let us know what's covered under your plan."

**Round 3:** **Trainer:** "This is so confusing. Will I have to pay for it or not?"

**Learner expected response:** "I understand this is frustrating. Your final cost depends on your insurance coverage, deductible, and co-insurance. I recommend calling your insurance at \[number on card\] to get a specific estimate for this service. You can also ask our billing office about our financial assistance programs or payment plans if you have concerns about cost. We'll do our best to work with your insurance and let you know the outcome."

**Checklist: Medical Necessity and Coverage Distinction**

* \[ \] Understands medical necessity (clinical appropriateness) ≠ coverage (payer payment)  
* \[ \] Identifies potential denial risks in diagnosis-service pairings  
* \[ \] Escalates coverage questions to authorization team  
* \[ \] Never promises coverage to patients  
* \[ \] Uses safe language ("submitted for authorization," "depends on your policy")  
* \[ \] Distinguishes between submission and approval  
* \[ \] Accesses coverage policies correctly (if applicable to role)  
* \[ \] Documents denial risk flags appropriately

**Scoring Rubric:**

* Denial risk prediction (12 scenarios): 4 points (10/12 \= 83%)  
* Patient communication safe language: 3 points  
* Medical necessity vs coverage distinction: 2 points  
* Escalation judgment: 1 point  
* **Total: 10 points (8/10 \= 80% passing)**

#### **6\) Knowledge Check (20 Items)**

**Item 1 (MCQ):** Medical necessity means: A) Insurance will pay for the service  
 B) Service is clinically appropriate for the diagnosis  
 C) Patient wants the service  
 D) Doctor ordered the service

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Medical necessity \= clinical appropriateness; separate from coverage determination.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — medical necessity ≠ coverage guarantee.

**Item 2 (MCQ):** Coverage means: A) Doctor says service is needed  
 B) Service is medically necessary  
 C) Payer will pay based on policy terms  
 D) Service is clinically appropriate

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** Coverage \= payer determination of payment based on policy.

**Item 3 (True/False):** If a service is medically necessary, insurance must cover it.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Service can be medically necessary but not covered by policy (source failure mode: confusing medical necessity vs coverage).  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — this is the critical misconception the module addresses.

**Item 4 (MCQ):** LCD stands for: A) Local Coverage Decision  
 B) Local Coverage Determination  
 C) Limited Coverage Database  
 D) Licensed Coverage Document

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Local Coverage Determination is the correct term.

**Item 5 (Scenario MCQ):** Patient asks "Will my insurance cover this MRI?" You should respond: A) "Yes, the doctor ordered it."  
 B) "Coverage depends on your policy. I've submitted for authorization."  
 C) "It's medically necessary so insurance has to pay."  
 D) "I guarantee it will be covered."

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Safe language avoids coverage guarantee while explaining process.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A or C — conflating medical necessity with coverage.

**Item 6 (MCQ):** Diagnosis-service pairing shows: Brain MRI with diagnosis "Diabetes." This is: A) Appropriate pairing  
 B) Potential denial risk; diagnosis doesn't match service body system  
 C) Automatically denied  
 D) No concern

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Diabetes diagnosis doesn't obviously support brain imaging; potential documentation gap or mismatch requiring investigation.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — not recognizing mismatch.

**Item 7 (True/False):** Your role in medical necessity determination is to decide whether the service is clinically appropriate for the diagnosis.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Medical necessity determination is provider's clinical role; operational staff flag potential coverage issues, not make medical necessity judgments.

**Item 8 (MCQ):** When you identify potential denial risk in diagnosis-service pairing, you should: A) Deny the service  
 B) Call the patient and tell them it won't be covered  
 C) Escalate to authorization team for policy review  
 D) Change the diagnosis code to match the service

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** Flag for expert review; don't independently determine denial or change codes.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** D — changing diagnosis codes exceeds non-coder boundary.

**Item 9 (MCQ):** Unspecified diagnosis codes (ending in .9) for high-cost services create: A) No concern  
 B) Increased denial risk due to lack of specificity  
 C) Automatic approval  
 D) Faster processing

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Unspecified codes lack detail payers need for medical necessity review.

**Item 10 (Scenario MCQ):** Claim denied because "diagnosis does not support medical necessity per LCD." This means: A) Doctor made medical error  
 B) Coder assigned wrong code  
 C) Payer's coverage policy doesn't list this diagnosis as covered indication for this service  
 D) Service was not medically necessary

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** LCD coverage criteria not met; separate from whether service was clinically appropriate (Source Scenario 19 addresses this distinction).  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** D — denial doesn't necessarily mean service wasn't medically necessary.

**Item 11 (MCQ):** Prior authorization is: A) Automatic approval for all services  
 B) Payer's advance review to confirm coverage before service delivery  
 C) Provider's permission to perform service  
 D) Patient's agreement to treatment

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Prior auth \= payer's advance coverage determination.

**Item 12 (True/False):** If a provider determines a service is medically necessary, the insurance company must accept that determination and pay the claim.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Payers make independent coverage determinations based on policy; provider's medical necessity judgment doesn't obligate payment.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — not understanding payer independence in coverage decisions.

**Item 13 (MCQ):** When patient asks about coverage, safe language includes: A) "It's covered, the doctor ordered it."  
 B) "I've submitted for authorization. Coverage depends on your policy."  
 C) "Don't worry, insurance always covers this."  
 D) "Definitely covered."

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Safe language avoids guarantees, explains process, acknowledges policy dependence.

**Item 14 (Scenario MCQ):** Order for physical therapy with diagnosis "Diabetes." What do you do? A) Proceed normally, diagnosis is fine  
 B) Flag potential mismatch (diabetes doesn't obviously support PT need), escalate for review  
 C) Deny the order  
 D) Change diagnosis to musculoskeletal code

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Diagnosis-service mismatch requires investigation; possible documentation gap.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — not recognizing body system mismatch.

**Item 15 (MCQ):** NCD stands for: A) New Coverage Directive  
 B) National Coverage Determination  
 C) Necessary Coverage Document  
 D) Non-Covered Diagnosis

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** National Coverage Determination is correct term for Medicare national policies.

**Item 16 (True/False):** You can guarantee coverage if the diagnosis code appears on the payer's covered diagnosis list for that service.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Even with covered diagnosis, other factors affect coverage (frequency limits, documentation requirements, patient's specific policy terms); cannot guarantee.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — oversimplifying coverage determination.

**Item 17 (MCQ):** If payer LCD is unclear about whether diagnosis supports service, you should: A) Interpret it yourself using best judgment  
 B) Escalate to authorization specialist for policy interpretation  
 C) Assume it's covered  
 D) Deny the service

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Policy interpretation requires expertise; escalate ambiguous cases.

**Item 18 (Scenario MCQ):** Patient: "My doctor says this test is medically necessary." You respond: A) "Then insurance will cover it."  
 B) "Medical necessity and coverage are separate. I've submitted for authorization; the payer will review based on your policy."  
 C) "Doctors don't determine coverage."  
 D) "You'll definitely have to pay."

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Educates on distinction while remaining helpful and professional.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — conflating medical necessity with coverage.

**Item 19 (Short Answer):** Explain the difference between medical necessity and coverage in one sentence each.

**Answer:**  
 Medical necessity: Service is clinically appropriate for the diagnosis based on standards of care (provider determination).  
 Coverage: Payer will pay for the service based on policy terms and coverage criteria (payer determination).

**Item 20 (MCQ):** Source Scenario 19 teaches that denial due to "diagnosis not covered per LCD" means: A) Coding error occurred  
 B) Service wasn't medically necessary  
 C) Coverage policy doesn't support this diagnosis-service combination  
 D) All of the above

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** LCD denial reflects payer's coverage policy, not necessarily coding error or lack of medical necessity.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — assuming denial always means coding error rather than coverage limitation.

#### **7\) QA and Audit Hooks**

**Evidence of Competence:**

* Prior auth prediction scenarios (12 items) scored for denial risk identification  
* Patient communication scripts (3 scenarios) scored for safe language use  
* Knowledge check (20 items, 16/20 \= 80%)

**Supervisor Review Checklist:**

* \[ \] Learner distinguishes medical necessity from coverage  
* \[ \] Learner identifies denial risks appropriately  
* \[ \] Learner escalates coverage questions (doesn't make independent coverage determinations)  
* \[ \] Learner uses safe language with patients (no coverage guarantees)  
* \[ \] Learner accesses coverage policies correctly (if applicable to role)

**Recordkeeping Tie-Back:**

* Module 7 completion with all exercises logged  
* Patient communication safety scores tracked  
* Module 7 scores recorded in training record

## **COURSE 3: DAY-TO-DAY DOCUMENT HYGIENE AND DATA HANDLING**

### **MODULE 5: SCREENSHOT PROHIBITION AND ALTERNATIVES (45 MIN)**

#### **1\) Entry-Level Primer**

**Plain Language Summary:** Screenshots of PHI screens are absolutely prohibited regardless of purpose, urgency, or recipient. Screenshots create uncontrolled PHI copies that persist on devices, can be accidentally shared, and are difficult to secure or delete permanently. This module trains alternative methods for troubleshooting, training, and communication that don't require screenshots: verbal descriptions, test environment demonstrations, approved screen sharing with proper controls, ticketing system descriptions, and sanitized template captures from non-PHI screens.

**Observable Behaviors:**

* Never takes screenshots of PHI screens under any circumstance  
* Uses verbal description or approved screen sharing for troubleshooting  
* Uses test environment or training environment for demonstrations  
* Escalates if supervisor or colleague requests screenshot  
* Reports if screenshot was taken accidentally (immediate deletion \+ incident report)

#### **2\) Definitions**

**Screenshot:** Digital image capture of screen content using built-in tools (Snipping Tool, Print Screen, screenshot apps) or phone camera. Creates permanent image file.

**PHI Screen:** Any display showing patient identifiers (name, MRN, account number, DOB, etc.) combined with health or payment context. Includes EHR screens, billing systems, claims platforms, authorization portals, patient portals, scheduling systems when patient data is visible.

**Screenshot Prohibition:** Absolute policy forbidding screenshots of PHI screens regardless of: purpose (training, troubleshooting, documentation), recipient (supervisor, colleague, IT), device (work computer, personal phone), or urgency. No exceptions.

**Phone Camera Capture:** Taking photo of computer screen using phone camera; included in screenshot prohibition (same PHI control failure).

**Approved Screen Sharing:** Real-time display of screen via approved collaboration tools (Teams, Zoom) with proper session controls (password protection, participant verification, recording disabled or controlled). Distinct from screenshot because temporary display, not permanent file creation.

**Test Environment / Training Environment:** Non-production system with fake patient data or no patient data, used for training and testing without PHI exposure. Screenshots may be allowed in test environment per policy (needs\_review).

**Verbal Description:** Explaining screen content or navigation issue without visual capture. Example: "On the Claims Search screen, I enter account number in the top field, click Search, but the results grid shows 'no records found' even though I know this account exists."

**Sanitized Template:** Screenshot of blank screen template from test environment or documentation, with no actual patient data visible. May be used for training if approved and verified PHI-free.

#### **3\) Step-by-Step Procedures**

**Procedure: Troubleshoot System Issues Without Screenshots**

**Scenario: System not working as expected, need help from IT or colleague**

1. **Describe the issue verbally or in writing:**

   * What screen/module are you in? (name of screen, not screenshot)  
   * What action are you attempting? (button clicked, field entered)  
   * What happens? (error message text, unexpected behavior)  
   * What do you expect to happen?  
2. **Example description for IT ticket:** "I'm in the Authorization Request module. I enter account number in the 'Account Number' field, select service type 'MRI' from dropdown, enter requested date, and click 'Submit.' The system displays error message: 'Invalid service type for this account.' I expected the request to submit successfully. This worked yesterday for similar accounts."

3. **If verbal/written description insufficient:**

   * Use approved screen sharing tool (needs\_review per client):  
     * Schedule Teams/Zoom call with IT or colleague  
     * Share screen in real-time (not screenshot)  
     * Demonstrate issue live while other party watches  
     * Verify session is password-protected and participants are authorized  
   * OR: Request IT remote access session where IT views your screen directly via support software  
4. **NEVER:**

   * Take screenshot and attach to email  
   * Take screenshot and attach to ticket  
   * Take phone photo of screen  
   * Share screenshot via any messaging platform  
5. **If colleague or supervisor requests screenshot:**

   * Refuse professionally: "Our policy prohibits screenshots of PHI screens. I can describe the issue verbally, schedule a screen share session, or you can view my screen directly."  
   * If pressure continues: escalate to compliance (safe stop Rule 4\)

**Procedure: Provide Training or Demonstrate Process Without Screenshots**

**Scenario: Need to train colleague on how to use system feature**

1. **Use test environment for demonstration:**

   * Access client's training or test environment (if available)  
   * Perform demonstration with fake/test patient data  
   * Trainee follows along in their test environment  
   * If screenshot needed for training materials: take screenshot from test environment only, verify no real PHI present  
2. **If test environment unavailable, use approved alternatives:**

   * **Live demonstration with screen sharing:** Schedule session, share screen via approved tool, trainee watches and takes notes  
   * **Verbal walkthrough with written steps:** Create step-by-step written instructions ("Click \[button name\], enter \[field description\], select \[option\]")  
   * **Use existing training documentation:** Reference official training guides, videos, or documentation from vendor or client training library  
3. **For training materials creation:**

   * Use test environment screenshots only (verify PHI-free)  
   * OR use blank templates from system documentation  
   * OR use screen captures from vendor training environment  
   * Never use production environment with real PHI for training materials  
4. **NEVER:**

   * Take screenshot of production screen with real patient data "as example"  
   * Share "sanitized" screenshot that might still contain hidden PHI  
   * Assume "just one screenshot for training" is acceptable exception

**Procedure: Respond to Accidental Screenshot Creation**

**If you accidentally take screenshot before realizing:**

1. **Immediate actions:**

   * Delete screenshot file immediately from all locations (screenshot folder, recycle bin, cloud sync folders)  
   * Empty recycle bin / permanently delete  
   * If screenshot was on phone: delete from phone and "Recently Deleted" folder  
2. **Report as incident:**

   * Notify supervisor immediately: "I accidentally took screenshot of PHI screen. I have deleted it completely. Reporting as incident."  
   * Complete incident report per Module 10 procedure  
   * Document: what screen, why screenshot was taken (accidental key press, mistaken troubleshooting attempt), deletion confirmation, device used  
3. **If screenshot was shared before realizing error:**

   * Follow Module 10 incident response  
   * Attempt to recall/delete from recipient (if email)  
   * Notify recipient to delete and not forward  
   * Escalate as potential PHI disclosure incident  
   * Preserve evidence (do not delete sent messages documenting the incident)  
4. **Do NOT:**

   * Keep screenshot "just in case"  
   * Keep screenshot thinking "I already took it, might as well use it"  
   * Keep screenshot with plan to "sanitize it later"

**Safe Stop Rules:**

* Stop if anyone asks you to take screenshot of PHI screen — refuse and offer alternatives  
* Stop if you instinctively reach for screenshot tool — pause, use verbal description instead  
* Stop if you see colleague taking screenshots — remind them of prohibition and offer to help find alternative  
* Stop if you accidentally take screenshot — delete immediately and report as incident

**Escalation Path:** Supervisor (for alternative method guidance) → IT (for screen sharing or remote access setup) → Compliance (if pressure to violate policy)

**Documentation Requirements:**

* Accidental screenshots documented as incidents (Module 10\)  
* Refusal to take screenshots documented if pressure occurred (safe stop Rule 4 documentation)  
* Alternative methods used documented in tickets/notes ("Demonstrated via screen share session" instead of screenshot attachment)

#### **4\) Worked Examples**

**Compliant Example 1 \- IT Troubleshooting Without Screenshot:** Employee encounters error in billing system. Employee's initial instinct is to screenshot error for IT ticket. Employee stops, remembers prohibition.

Employee creates IT ticket: "I'm in Billing module, Claims Search screen. I enter account number 789456 in the Account Number field (top left), click 'Search' button (top right). System displays error message 'Database connection timeout.' I expect the search results to populate in the grid below. This error occurs consistently for the past 30 minutes across multiple account searches."

IT responds: "Can you schedule a screen share so I can watch you reproduce the error?"

Employee schedules Teams meeting, shares screen in real-time, reproduces error while IT watches. IT identifies configuration issue and resolves.

**Why Compliant:** Used verbal description in ticket, accepted screen share invitation as appropriate alternative, no screenshot created.

**Compliant Example 2 \- Training New Colleague:** Experienced employee asked to show new hire how to process authorization requests.

Employee: "Let's use the training environment so I can demonstrate without using real patient data."

Logs into training environment with fake patient records, walks new hire through entire authorization request process using test data. New hire practices in training environment.

For documentation, employee uses existing training guide screenshots (from training environment, verified PHI-free by training department).

**Why Compliant:** Used test environment for demonstration, referenced approved training materials, no production PHI screens captured.

**Non-Compliant Example (Source Scenarios 5 and Course 3 Scenario 8 Referenced):** Colleague asks: "I'm having trouble with this screen. Can you just take a screenshot and send it to me so I can see what you're seeing?"

Employee: "Sure, here you go." \[Takes screenshot of EHR screen showing patient name, MRN, DOB, diagnosis, and sends via email\]

**Why Non-Compliant:**

* Violated screenshot prohibition (absolute rule, no exceptions)  
* Created uncontrolled PHI copy (screenshot file)  
* Shared PHI via email attachment (additional exposure)  
* Source Scenario 5 explicitly addresses screenshot prohibition  
* Course 3 Scenario 8 reinforces (Order Entry Specialist scenario involves screenshot violation)

**Correct response should have been:** "Our policy prohibits screenshots of PHI screens. I can schedule a quick screen share with you, or I can describe what I'm seeing. What works better for you?"

**Result if violation discovered:** Incident report required, likely sanctions (counseling at minimum, potentially written warning or higher depending on content sensitivity and prior history), mandatory Module 5 refresher, possible restriction on system access pending investigation.

#### **5\) Practice Pack**

**Drill Set: Screenshot vs Alternative Decision (10 Scenarios)**

For each scenario, identify: (A) Screenshot prohibited, use \[alternative method\], or (B) Screenshot may be allowed (if test environment/blank template)

1. Need to show colleague how to complete form. Production screen with patient data visible. → A \- PROHIBITED (use test environment demonstration or verbal walkthrough)

2. Need to document system error for IT. Error message appears on patient record screen. → A \- PROHIBITED (copy error message text verbally, use screen share, or describe error)

3. Creating training manual. Want to show screen layout. Use training environment with no real patients. → B \- MAY BE ALLOWED (verify training environment is PHI-free, confirm training materials policy allows)

4. Supervisor asks "Can you screenshot that denial code so I can see what you're talking about?" Denial on patient claim screen. → A \- PROHIBITED (describe denial code verbally: "Denial code CO-45: Charge exceeds fee schedule/maximum allowable")

5. Need to show IT where button is located. Button visible on screen with patient account data. → A \- PROHIBITED (describe button location verbally: "Button is top right corner, labeled 'Submit Auth'")

6. Creating job aid for team. Want to show sample workflow. Production screen. → A \- PROHIBITED (use test environment, blank templates from documentation, or text-based instructions)

7. Vendor asks for screenshot to troubleshoot software issue. Patient data visible. → A \- PROHIBITED (describe to vendor or offer screen share session with vendor support; never send screenshot with PHI)

8. Accidentally hit Print Screen key while patient record open. → A \- SCREENSHOT CREATED \- DELETE IMMEDIATELY, REPORT AS INCIDENT

9. Want to show example of correct form completion for training. Blank form template from documentation library, no patient data. → B \- MAY BE ALLOWED (verify completely blank/template)

10. Colleague texts "Send me screenshot of the error." Error on screen with patient MRN visible. → A \- PROHIBITED (explain prohibition to colleague, offer alternatives)

**Scoring:** 100% required (10/10 correct) \- screenshot prohibition is absolute, no partial credit

**Scenario Exercise: Colleague Requests Screenshot**

**Scenario:** Colleague messages you: "Hey, I'm stuck on the authorization screen. Can you take a screenshot of your screen and show me where the Submit button is? I can't find it."

**Your response (write exact words):**

**Sample Compliant Response:** "I can't send screenshots per our policy, but I can help\! The Submit button is in the bottom right corner of the authorization form, below all the diagnosis fields. It's labeled 'Submit for Review.' Does that help? If not, I can do a quick screen share and show you live, or we can hop on a call and I'll walk you through it."

**Why Compliant:**

* Refuses screenshot clearly but helpfully  
* Provides verbal description of button location  
* Offers alternatives (screen share, phone walkthrough)  
* Maintains compliance while being supportive colleague

**Non-Compliant Response:** "Sure, here's a screenshot." \[Sends screenshot of patient authorization screen\]

**Why Non-Compliant:** Violates absolute prohibition; creates incident.

**Mini-Simulation Script:** **Trainer plays:** Various requesters  
 **Learner plays:** Employee refusing screenshots and offering alternatives

**Round 1 \- Peer Request:** **Trainer:** "Can you screenshot that screen for me?"

**Learner expected response:** "I can't send screenshots of PHI screens per policy. I can describe what I'm seeing, share my screen in a meeting, or you can remote into my workstation if you have access. Which works for you?"

**Round 2 \- Supervisor Request:** **Trainer (as supervisor):** "I need you to screenshot that denial and email it to me right away."

**Learner expected response:** "Our policy prohibits screenshots of PHI screens. I can forward you the denial details in the ticket, schedule a screen share so you can see it directly, or describe the denial code and reason. Would one of those work?"

**Round 3 \- Urgent Pressure:** **Trainer:** "This is urgent\! Just take a quick screenshot, no one will know\!"

**Learner expected response:** "I understand the urgency, but I cannot take screenshots of PHI screens under any circumstance, including urgent situations. Let's find an alternative solution right now. Can you view my screen remotely or can I read you the information you need?"

\[If pressure continues, learner escalates to compliance per safe stop Rule 4\]

**Checklist: Screenshot Prohibition Compliance**

* \[ \] Never takes screenshots of PHI screens  
* \[ \] Uses verbal descriptions for troubleshooting  
* \[ \] Uses approved screen sharing for demonstrations  
* \[ \] Uses test environment for training when possible  
* \[ \] Refuses screenshot requests professionally  
* \[ \] Offers alternatives when screenshot requested  
* \[ \] Deletes immediately if accidental screenshot occurs  
* \[ \] Reports accidental screenshots as incidents  
* \[ \] Escalates if pressured to violate prohibition

**Scoring Rubric:**

* Decision drill (10 scenarios): 5 points (10/10 \= 100% required for screenshot prohibition absolutes)  
* Refusal script: 3 points (clear refusal \+ alternatives offered)  
* Alternative methods knowledge: 2 points  
* **Total: 10 points (10/10 \= 100% passing for this critical safety module)**

#### **6\) Knowledge Check (15 Items)**

**Item 1 (MCQ):** Screenshots of PHI screens are: A) Allowed for training purposes  
 B) Allowed if sent to supervisor  
 C) Allowed in emergencies  
 D) Absolutely prohibited with no exceptions

**Answer:** D  
 **Rationale:** Screenshot prohibition is absolute; no exceptions for purpose, recipient, or urgency.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — training does not exempt; use test environment.

**Item 2 (MCQ):** Appropriate alternative to screenshot for IT troubleshooting: A) Take screenshot but encrypt it  
 B) Use phone camera instead  
 C) Verbal description or approved screen sharing  
 D) Screenshot and immediately delete after sending

**Answer:** C  
 **Rationale:** Verbal description or screen sharing provides help without creating uncontrolled PHI file.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — encryption doesn't address uncontrolled copy issue.

**Item 3 (True/False):** If supervisor requests screenshot of patient screen, you must comply since they're your supervisor.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** Screenshot prohibition applies regardless of requester; refuse and offer alternatives.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — not understanding that policy overrides supervisor request.

**Item 4 (MCQ):** You accidentally take screenshot of patient record screen. What do you do? A) Keep it but don't share it  
 B) Delete immediately, report as incident  
 C) Save for later in case needed  
 D) Sanitize patient info and keep template

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Immediate deletion and incident reporting required.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** D — "sanitizing" screenshot is not acceptable; delete entirely.

**Item 5 (Scenario MCQ):** Colleague asks you to screenshot error message showing on patient claim screen. You should: A) Take screenshot and send it  
 B) Refuse; describe error message text verbally instead  
 C) Take screenshot but blur patient info  
 D) Screenshot only the error message area

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Refuse screenshot (prohibited); describe error message text as alternative.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** D — "only part of screen" doesn't eliminate prohibition if PHI present anywhere on screen.

**Item 6 (MCQ):** Test environment screenshots are: A) Always allowed  
 B) May be allowed if verified PHI-free and policy permits  
 C) Prohibited like production screenshots  
 D) Only allowed for IT

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Test environment screenshots may be permitted if no real PHI and training materials policy allows (needs\_review).  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — still requires verification of PHI absence and policy check.

**Item 7 (True/False):** Phone camera photos of computer screens are included in screenshot prohibition.

**Answer:** True  
 **Rationale:** Phone camera capture creates same uncontrolled PHI copy problem; included in prohibition.

**Item 8 (MCQ):** Approved screen sharing differs from screenshot because: A) It's the same thing  
 B) It's temporary real-time display, not permanent file creation  
 C) It requires password  
 D) Only supervisors can screen share

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Screen sharing is temporary display with session controls; screenshot creates permanent file.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — password is control feature but not the core distinction.

**Item 9 (Scenario MCQ):** You need to train new hire on authorization process. Best method: A) Screenshot each step from production environment  
 B) Use test environment demonstration with fake patient data  
 C) Screenshot production but remove patient names  
 D) Video record your screen with production data

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Test environment allows demonstration without real PHI exposure.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — "removing names" doesn't fully address screenshot prohibition and leaves other identifiers.

**Item 10 (MCQ):** Vendor asks for screenshot to troubleshoot software issue affecting patient record display. You should: A) Provide screenshot since vendor needs it  
 B) Refuse; offer verbal description or screen share session with vendor support  
 C) Screenshot and redact patient info first  
 D) Get supervisor permission then screenshot

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Prohibition applies to vendor requests; use alternatives.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — vendor need doesn't override prohibition.

**Item 11 (True/False):** "Just one quick screenshot for urgent troubleshooting" is an acceptable exception to the prohibition.

**Answer:** False  
 **Rationale:** No exceptions for urgency; prohibition is absolute.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** True — rationalizing exception for urgent situations.

**Item 12 (MCQ):** Source Scenarios 5 and Course 3 Scenario 8 address: A) Password sharing  
 B) Screenshot prohibition violations  
 C) Email security  
 D) Access controls

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Both scenarios explicitly address screenshot violations and appropriate alternatives.

**Item 13 (Scenario MCQ):** You see colleague taking screenshot of patient billing screen. What do you do? A) Nothing, not your concern  
 B) Remind colleague of prohibition and offer to help find alternative method  
 C) Report colleague immediately without speaking to them  
 D) Take screenshot yourself to show them how it's done

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Peer accountability; remind of policy and help find compliant alternative.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** C — direct intervention and education before formal reporting is appropriate for peer violations.

**Item 14 (Short Answer):** List three alternatives to screenshots for troubleshooting or training.

**Answer:** (Any three of: verbal description, approved screen sharing, test environment demonstration, remote access by IT/supervisor, written step-by-step instructions, existing training documentation/videos, blank templates from vendor documentation)

**Item 15 (MCQ):** Screenshot prohibition exists because screenshots: A) Take up storage space  
 B) Create uncontrolled PHI copies that persist, can be accidentally shared, and are difficult to secure or permanently delete  
 C) Violate copyright  
 D) Slow down computers

**Answer:** B  
 **Rationale:** Core security concern is uncontrolled PHI file creation and persistence.  
 **Common Wrong Answer:** A — storage is minor concern compared to PHI control issue.

#### **7\) QA and Audit Hooks**

**Evidence of Competence:**

* Decision drill (10 scenarios, 10/10 \= 100% required)  
* Refusal script demonstrations (3 scenarios)  
* Alternative method knowledge assessment  
* Knowledge check (15 items, 15/15 \= 100% for this critical safety module)

**Supervisor Review Checklist:**

* \[ \] Learner demonstrates zero tolerance for screenshots  
* \[ \] Learner can articulate alternative methods for common scenarios  
* \[ \] Learner responds appropriately to screenshot requests (refuses \+ offers alternatives)  
* \[ \] Learner understands incident response if accidental screenshot occurs  
* \[ \] Learner understands test environment may allow screenshots if PHI-free and policy permits

**Recordkeeping Tie-Back:**

* Module 5 completion with 100% passing recorded (critical safety module)  
* Screenshot prohibition acknowledgment signed separately as high-risk policy  
* Any screenshot incidents documented separately per Module 10 (breach of critical control)
